SOCIALIST FEDERAL REPUBLIC OF YUGOSLAVIA
E;TRIA AND SLOVENE COAST WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrowers:               Rizana Water Works (RWW), Istrian Water Works (IWW),
and Pula Water Works (PWW)
Guarantor:               Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia
Loan Amnounts:           RWW - $32 million equivalent; IWW and PWW - $28
million equivalent
Terms:                   Fifteen years, including 5 years grace, at the
standard variable interest rate
Project Description:     The Project comprises two components, the Slovene
Coast component and the Istria component, financed by
two separate Bank loans. Both components include
water and sewerage werks. The water works would be
implemented and financed by the three Borrowers, and
the sewerage works would be implemented and financed
by eight of the nine communities in the project area
and a regional water communitiy of interest. Bank
loan funds would not be used for the sewerage works.
The Project would in the Service Areas of the
Borrowers: (i) eliminate existing water shortages;
(ii) provide additional water supply capacity needed
for expansion of the tourism industry and growth in
domestic and industrial/commercial demand; (iii)
reduce unaccounted-for water; (iv) protect existing
water sources and coastal tourist areas from
pollution; and (v) increase efficiency in the delivery
of water supply and sewerage services.
Benefits:                The water works in the Project would provide a safe
and sufficient water supply to the households, tourism
facilities, productive enterprises and social
facilities in the project area; all of which have
experienced shortages during the past three summers.
The sewerage works would insure that the increased
sewage resulting from the Project would be disposed of
in an environmentally sound manner. Justification for
the Project is based on the foreign exchange earnings
of tourism in the project area, which would decline
without the Project, and on the positive impact of the
sewerage works in the Project on the environment.
Riskm                    There are two potential risks.  One is the risk of
possible damage to the area's tourism development if
the water shortages are not managed properly until the
Project is completed. At the present timo, the
available water is being distributed equitably and



- ii -
efficiently. The Bank would try to minimize this risk
by mmnitoring operational procedures of the three
Water Works as part of its supervision. The other is
a risk that the overall investment climate in
Yugoslavia could deteriorate further and become a
constraint to tourism development in the project
area. However, the demand for tourism services is
likely to remain strong even in the presence of
negative macro-economic trends.

SOCIALIST FEDERAL REPUBLIC OF YUGOSLAVIA
ISTRIA AND SLOVENE COAST WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. PROJECT ORIGIN AND SETTING
The Project and Its Or 0
1.01     The proposed Project would expand water and sewer system capacity in
seven coastal communities and two inland communities in the northern Adriatic
Coastal region of Yugoslavia. Three of the Communities are located in the
Republic of Slovenia and the other six are in the Istrian peninsula of the
Croatian Republic. There are two independent project components (The Slovene
Coast component and the Istria component), three Borrowers (the Rizana Water
Works, the Istrian Water Works and the Pula Water Works), and twelve
implementing agencies (the Rizana Water Works, the Butoniga Water Works, two
community infrastructure works enterprises, six local infrastructure
communities of interest and one regional water community of interest).
Separate loans are proposed for each of the two project components. Two of
the proposed Borrowers (the Istria Water Works and the Pula Water Works) would
be jointly and severally responsible for one of the loans. The main purpose
of the Project is to provide water and sewerage services in support of the
growing tourism industry in the region, which is a major foreign exchange
earner for Yugoslavia.
1.02     The Project has been in the planning and development stage by local
authorities since about 1983 when water shortages during the summer tourist
season first became noticeable. Bank involvement began with a request from
the Federal Secretariat for Finance in August 1987 to review the Project for
possible Bank lending. In response to this request, a Bank identification
mission visited the two implementing agencies, the Rizana Water Works and the
Butoniga Water Works, in September 1987. Project preparation began with a
mission in December 1987. Due to the critical nature of the shortage, project
implementation is already in progress, having started in mid-1987 for both
project components. The Bank will only participate in the financing of water
works contracted after June 1, 1988.
1.03     The sewerage works were added to the Project during preparation at
the suggestion of the Bank to insure that the sewage resulting from the
additional water supply capacity would be disposed of in an environmentally
sound manner. Most of the sewerage works were already in the investment
programs of the benefitting communities. For the most part, they will be
implemented and financed by them. One high priority works would be financed
and implemented by the Republic of Croatia because of its regional importance
and high cost. The Bank loans would not be used to finance the sewerage works
since local funds for these works have alLeady been committed and the Water
Works, who are the Borrowers and are not responsible for sewerage, chose not
to borrow from the Bank on behalf of the communities.



-2-
Country Background
1.04     The Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia consists of six
republics: Serbia, Croatia, Slovenia, Bosnia-Hercegovina, Macedonia and
Montenegro and two autonomous provinces, Vojvodina and Kosovo which are part
of Serbia. Total area is 255,800 sq. km with 2,092 km of coastline along the
Adriatic. The population of Yugoslavia at the end of 1987 was 23.4 mill:on,
with 48% living in urban areas. The population growth rate is 0.7% per annum.
1.05     Yugoslavia is a middle income country with a per capita GDP vf
approximately US$2,477 in 1987. During the 1970s the economy grew rapidly,
with the growth of GDP averaging 6% per year. This performance was supported
by large investments but insufficient attention was paid by policy-makers and
investors to their quality and cost-efficiency. The increasing burden of
debt-service, the recession in OECD countries and the increasing reluctance of
international creditors to lend to Yugoslavia in the 1980s forced Yugoslavia
to initiate measures to reduce domestic demands and improve the efficiency of
resources. However, these measures were not consistently implemented. GDP
more or less stagnated between 1980-1987 and domestic inflation (as measured
by the ;rowth of the retail price index) rose from an average rate of 202 in
the 1976s to 118% in 1987. Faced with a critical foreign exchange liquidity
crisis, stagnant growth and accelerating inflation, in May 1988 the Government
initiated a strong program to cantrol inflation and to correct important
structural weaknesses in the economy. The IMF supported this program with a
Standby Arrangement in June 1988. A second structural adjustment loan from
the Bank in support of the adjustment program is in an advanced stage of
preparation.
The Project Area
1.06     The project area is located at the northern end of the Adriatic
adjacent to the Italian border. One-third of the area is in the Republic of
Slovenia and two-thirds in the Republic of Croatia. It includes the Slovene
Coastal Region consisting of the communities of Koper, Izola and Piran and the
west coast of the Istrian peninsula consisting of the Croatian communities of
Buje, Porec, Rovinj, Buzet, Pazin and Pula. The permanent population numbers
242,000; however, during the summer months, the population doubles with the
arrival of temporary residents and tourists. The area's economy is dominated
by tourism and the shipbuilding industry, with tourism providing about 25% of
the gross domestic product. The area is popular with foreign tourists because
of its proximity to Western Europe, its competitive prices, its modern
facilities, and natural assets which so far have not been overdeveloped.
Bank Assistance Strateg
1.07     The main thrust of the Bank's assistance strategy in Yugoslavia is to
support the country's ongoing economic reform. Since the early 1980s, the
Government has embarked on a long-term economic adjustment and reform program
intended to give the economy a greater market-based orientation, with a view
to the Europe of 1992 and beyond. This effort was supported in FY83 by the
Bank's first SAL to Yugoslavia, as part of an international financial support



pi,ckage which also included two IMF Standby Arrangements. However, since the
implementation of the early reform efforts was uneven and lacked sufficient
determination, the economy slipped back into a state of stagflation. In late
1987, the Yugoslav authorities announced   iew program of stabilization and
adjustment in an effort to reactivate the reform drive and give it more
substance and direction. By mid-1988, a new IMF Standby Arrangement in
support of this program was approved and rescheduling agreements were reached
with the Paris Club and Yugoslavia's najor commercial creditors. In the
context of the preparation of a second SAL, the Bank participated actively
with the Fund and the bilateral and commercial creditors in the discussion of
the Government's stabilization and adjustment program.
1.08     The proposed SAL II is expected to assist the reform drive by
deepening the adjustment measures already being implemented under the ongoing
Standby and by including several other important measures that would gernerate
and accelerate the required supply response by the real economy. It is
intended to be one of a series of policy-based operations in support of the
reform which together would cover a wide range of complementary policy areas
including price and trade liberalization, financial and fiscal sector reform,
the foreign exchange allocation system and enterprise reform. As such, the
proposed SAL II is seen as the linchpin of the Bank's strategy for intensified
assistance to Yugoslavia. Its approval and successful implementation would
pave the way for subsequent policy-based operations and would justify an
enlarged overall lending envelope to Yugoslavia which would also include
several sector and investment loans in industry, transport and power and
smaller investment projects in environmental improvement, water resource
management and possibly other areas of infrastructure. The operations in
industry would be primarily in support of promoting a supply response through
increased flexibility in the productive apparatus, focusing on
Lxport-orientation, small and medium-scale enterprise development and
enterprise rehabilitation on a selective basis. Subsector and investment
operations in transport and power would support the improvement of sectoral
policies, particularly pricing, investment choice and operational efficiency.
The smaller investment projects would consist of projects which essentially
stand on their own merit and whose developmental impart is not critically
dependent on overall policy reform. The proposed Project fits into this last
category.
Rationale for Bank Involvement
1.09     The Project would fill three important needs.  First, it is essential
to the tourism industry in an area where tourism is a major foreign exchange
earner. Second, pollution control works and studies included in the Project
would ensure that sewage resulting from increased water use made possible by
the Project would be disposed of in an envi:ronmentally sound manner. Third,
it would promote improvement in system operation and management through
studies, action plans for the reduction of unaccounted-for water and technical
assistance. The Bank's presence is essential since under Yugoslavia's present
economic environment, it would be difficult for the Water Works to secu.re
foreign financing for the Project without the Bank. More importantly, the
Bank would ensure that the pollution control issues associated with the
Project are adequately addressed.



11. THE TOURISM SECTOR
Tourism and the Economy
2.01     Since the early 1950s, when the first attempts to attract foreign
tourists were made, tourism has evolved into an important economic sector, a
major earner of foreign exchange, and an important generator of income and
employment. Over the last five years, tourism has accounted for close to 32
of Yugoslavia's social product, about 42 of employment, and about 10% of the
total value of exports.
2.02     Yugoslavia's share in European tourist traffic, measured in terms of
overnights, is about 4X, and it ranks fairly high among European destinations
in terms of nights spent by foreign tourists, with only Spain, Italy, Austria,
France, and the Unitad Kingdom registering more nights. Of these leading six
countries, Yugoslavia has one of the highest growth rates in foreign tourist
overnights, (at over 6.5% over the last decade).-/ The only other European
destinations with comparably high growth rates are Turkey and Portugal, which
still attract relatively small numbers of foreign overnights in comparison
with Yugoslavia (about 4.5 million and 15 million, respectively, versus 27
million).
2.03     Yugoslavia has competed successfully in the European tourism market
as a low-cost summer resort destination for group travel from Western Europe.
The major determinants are the relatively low cost of accommodation and
services, and low travel costs because of its geographical proximity to the
expanding European markets. Yugoslavia's attractions include 6,000 kmw' of
Adriatic coast, a Mediterranean climate, and an interesting variety of
cultural and historical assets.
2.04     Relatively few non-Europeans visit Yugoslavia, probably because of
the comparative attractiveness of destinations in France, Italy, Greece, and
the United Kingdom. West Germany has always been the dominating country of
origin, and demand from the neighboring countries of Austria and Italy has
been consistently strong. In recent years, tLere has been rapid growth in
tourists from the United Kingdom, which has now surpassed Austria, to become
the second country of origin.
2.05     Seasonality is particularly marked in Yugoslavian tourism, as is the
concentration of tourist overnights along the coast. Well over 90% of all
foreign tourism takes place in the three coastal republics of Slovenia,
Croatia and Montenegro, with about 80% in Croatia alone. Efforts are being
made to extend the tourism season and to diversify, but most activity will
probably continue to take place between May and October. Winiter tourism is
confined to specific centers.
1/ Annex 2.1 shows basic tourism statistics for Yugoslavia. Additional
background information on tourism is in the Project Files.
2/  Including islands.



2.06     Demand for toutrist accommodation in Yugoslavia still exceeds supply;
much of the coastline is still relatively undeveloped, and there is
considerable scope for further growth in nearly all kinds of accommodation.
Sector Organization
2.07     The larger tourist facilities in Yugoslavia, including most hotels
and apartment complexes and campsites, are constructed and operated by work
organizations. The number of rooms and apartments available to tourists in
private homes is increasing, however, and legislation is under consideration
which would encourage private investment in small boarding houses. The
organization and management of tourism sector enterprises is similar to that
of other work organizations in Yugoslavia; i.e., workers are members of the
organization, participating in planning, management, and economic
decision-making. There are, however, some joint venture hotels operating
successfully in Yugoslavia, and there are plans to allow a more diversified
range of ownership/management options in the future.
2.08     Work organizations in the sector generally consist of at least
several hotels. The larger ones include hotels, apartment complexes, and
campsites, usually in several different qua'Lity categories; some also act as
agents for private room or apartment rentals. Tourism work organizations are
often part of a larger enterprise, undertaking, for example, agricultural
production as well as tourism.
2.09     In addition, there are three different types of organizations which
influence the development of tourism in Yugoslavia; the government committee
at the community, republic, and federal levels, the chambers of economy, and
the tourist associations. Government committees for tourism are concerned
primarily with planning and legislative matters. Primary governmental
responsibility for tourism is at the republic level; tiowever, committees for
tourism only exist in republics where tourism is a major activity; in other
republics, tourism is subsumed within the committee for trade.
2.10     Tourist associations are non-governmental; at the commune level, they
include individuals, business enterprises, and other organizations interested
in tourism development in their areas. Members are volunteers, and represent
hotels, restaurants, handicraft shops, design and printing organizations,
etc. Their activities include tourism promotion, and making recommendations
to communes regarding, for example, improvement of tourist amenities and
infrastructure. Combinations of the commune level associations form the
regional associations, with similar activities, but with a regional focus; for
example, promoting regional entertainment events.   At the Federal level, the
Yugoslavia Tourist Association operates tourist offices in foreign countries,
and carries out publicity programs overseas.
2.11     Tourism planning is an integral part of macro-economic planning in
Yugoslavia. General guidelines are prepared at the republic level, and these
are transformed into operational plans at the commune level. Where
communities share common characteristics, there may be an additional
coordinating level. In Slovenia, for example, there is a coastal Community



Group, which has a research and coordination role in certain areas of concern
common to the three coastal communities, including water resources and roads.
In Croatia, the six communes of the project area fall under the larger Rijeka
planning region. Land-use planning and regulations are the responsibility of
local government, and is usually carried out in cooperation with Republic or
regional institutes or planning bureaus. Planning incorporates environmental
concerns; both project areas are part of a regional physical plan which
includes environmental protection as an essential element.



- 7 -
m. THE WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR
Sector Organizationi
3.01     The water supply and sewerage sector in Yugoslavia is organized by
government level and function. In general, regulatory requirements are
established at the federal level, water resource management and planning is a
republic and provincial responsibility, and the provision of services is a
local responsibility. There is no national water and sewerage authority.
3.02     The establishment of standards and guidelines is a federal function
that is carried out by the Federal Secretariat for Agriculture, Forestry and
Water Resources and the Federal Secretariat for Work, Health and Welfare. The
former is responsible for establishing guidelines for the classification
according to water quality of rivers, streams and lakes; the establishment of
wastewater discharge guidelines; and the settling of water rights issues that
may develop between two or more Republics or between Yugoslavia and its
neighbors. The latter is responsible for setting guidelines for potable water
quality. The establishment of regulations and their enforcement is a republic
function. The setting of specific standards for drinking water quality and
the monitoring of compliance are local functions.
3.03     The development and use of water resources is controlled mainly at
the republic level. In each republic, there is one organization with overall
responsibility for coordination and review of all water and sewage sector
investment. In Croatia, this organization is the Republican Committee for
Water Management. In Slovenia, it is the Republican Committee for the
Environment. Another type of republic organization is the water economy
enterprise. It is organized to implement and operate drainage, irrigation and
flood control schemes which benefit more than one community or interest
group. The Sava River Drainage Authority, established to implement an
irrigation and drainage project financed by loan 1756-YU, is an example of
this type of organization. This is the only organization of its type in
Croatia, There is none at present in Slovenia.
3.04     In between the republic level and the local level are water
organizations established to coordinate the allocation and development of
water resources within river basins. In Slovenia, they are Water
Associations, and in Croatia, they are Water Communities of Interest (WCI).
Each organization has a master plan for the development of water resources
within its area, and it must approve all investments to ensure they are in
keeping with the master plan. In Croatia there are eleven regional WCIs and
in Slovenia there are eight regional Water Associations.
3.05     Water is supplied to the public for domestic, industrial and
commercial purposes by self-managed enterprises, usually know as "vodovods"
(water works). These enterprises follow Yugoslavia's normal commercial
management principles and are financially autonomous. However, their
investment programs and tariffs are controlled by the communities they
serve, - and as mentioned above, the development of new water sources and
1/ A community in Yugoslavia is an administrative area, normally covering a
town and its surrounding rural area. It has an assembly and executive
council.



major investments must be approved at the association and republic level.
Typically there is a vodovod for each community, with a ceparate water source
being developed by each. However, as local sources become fully utilized, the
vodovods and community seek to develop water sources further afield. For this
purpose they find it necessary to enter into self-management agreements with
other communities and vodovods to form water communities of interest in order
to develop joint supplies. The communities of interest provide financing by
taxing the beneficiaries.
Water Supply and Sewerage Service Levels
3.06     Water supply and sewerage service levels in Yugoslavia are higi in
comparison with the Bank's other Borrowers, Average figures for 1986 are in
Table 3.1. Water supply coverage in urban areas averages 902. Sewerage
service levels in urban areas average 70X. Despite the high coverage, many
water systems lack capacity to meet peak summer demands and most sewerage
systems discharge waste water with little or no treatment to the nearest
stream, river or drainage channel.

Sector Investment and Financin
3.07     Investment in the sector in 1986 was estimated at about US$167
million, about 0.3% of GDP and 1.1% of gross investment. These percentages
have been fairly constant during the past five years with expenditures for
water supply averaging about 50% more than sewerage. Increased expenditures
for sewerage are expected in the future as the concern for the environment
grow.,.
3.08     The sector is unique in that it is self-financing.  Almost all
investment is financed by the users. Most financing originates from water
communities of interest (WCI). These are user groups which are formed to
raise financing for new investment, and in some cases, actually carry out the
works. Other common sources of financing are tariffs, self-imposed taxes
voted by the benefitting community, special funds raised through fines
collected from polluters and republic water communities of interest. Tariffs
are set normally only to cover operation, maintenance, depreciation and debt
service; however, for major investments, the beneficiaries through a WCI may



- 9 -
vote to add an investment surcharge to the tariffs for a fixed period of
time. Republic WCI funds are only available for investments serving more than
one community or that are judged to benefit the republic. Funds raised
through the collection of pollution fines are only available for the upgrading
of water treatment facilities using poiluted sources. Less common financing
sources are export credits, World Bank loans and tourist taxes. Loc:al,
republic and federal budgets are rarely used to finance investment.
Constraints to Sector Development
3.09     The main constraint to development of the water sector in Yugoslavia
is the shortage of financing for projects. Prior to 1982, there was little or
no problem of securing the approval of the population for a payroll assessment
for water supply system improvements. That willingness has diminished as a
result of recently accelerating inflation and restricted wage increases.
Also, there is a shortage of local long term credit so that project size is
restricted by the amount of cash that can be raised during proiect
implementation.   Other constraints are overly bureaucratic procedures for the
approval of new projects at all levels of government, and an artificial
constraint on the development of water resources involving more than one
republic imposed by the autonomy of the republics. Long lead times are needed
to develop projects in order to obtain local political support and the
required republic approvals. On occasion, uneconomic investments are made
because the least-cost solution would require the transport of water across
republic boundaries. None of these constraints are unique to the water sector
and the solutions are not apparent. The Bank's loans have helped overcome
these constraints through financing, pressing for approvals at the republic
and federal levels for projects and refusing to finance projects that are not
the least-cost solution.
World Bank Involvement in the Sector
3.10     The Bank has made the following loans in Yugoslavia for water supply
and sewerage:

Project
All projects have been completed and project completion reports issued with
the exception of Loan for the Tuzla Region Water Supply and Environmental
Project which is expected to be completed this year (1988). Several of the
projects had benefits outside the water and sewerage sector. Three of the
projects were multipurpose, and included irrigation, power and flood control



- 10 -
benefits. Others evolved as necessary complements to the solution of problems
outside the sector. Thus, the Dubrovnik project was associated with tourism
and the Tuzla project with air pollution control.
-.11     Bank experience has been that most projects are implemented -well and
require minimum supervision of the technical aspects. Contracting and
consultancy capability is very good, at all levels of project size. Technical
expertise provided by the Bank and outside consultants has been limited to a
few specialists where local consultants have not yet developed sufficient
expertise. Most of the Bank's assistance has focused on assisting with the
development of institutions, and preparing sound financial strategies. Bank
intervention, with substantial financing contributions, has often acted as a
catalyst in securing required local finance, which hitherto has been difficult
to obtain.
Bank Goals in the Sector
3.12     Past lending in the sector focused primarily on meeting basic needs
and improving health and has been limited to the lesser developed republics
and provinces where these needs are the greatest. This strategy was
successful and the basic water and sanitation needs of Yugoslavia's population
are by and large satisfied (para. 3.06). Emphasis in the future will be to
support -rojects that help to accelerate economic growth and improve the
environment. The proposed project falls in these categories.



- 11 -
IV. TOURISM AND THE DEMAND FOR TOURISM
FACILITIES IN THE PROJECT AREA
Existing Development
4.01     The project area is an important contributor to tourism, accounting
for about 32% of foreign tourist overnights spent in Yugoslavia. The project
area accounts for about 20% of the country's total tourist accommodations.
Hotels, motels, inns, and other permanent tourist settlements make up about
272 of the beds, another 42% are in campsites, and about 162 are in private
rooms. The reffaining beds are in youths' and workers' holiday camps, used
mostly by domestic tourists. Boat berths account for a small but growing
proportion of tourism capacity. The marinas in the area are considered to be
particularly profitable, but are already operating at capacity.
Potential for Future Development
4.02     There is a great deal of potential for further tourism development in
the project area. It is planned to keep the larger tourist facilities
generally grouped into settlements, rather than dispersed along the coast, in
orde- to leave some areas undeveloped, for the use of future generations.
Long-term plans are based on projections of the capacity of the region's
beaches as well as its infrastructure and manpower.  The plans for both the
Rijeka region (which contains the six Croatian project communities) and the
Slovene coastal region (containing three project communities) project growth
in capacity at just over 2% per year to 'he year 2000. This figure appears
reasonable in the light of past growth in supply (bed capacity), averaging
2.4% per year since 1980.
4.03     Growth in both demand and supply in the project area has been
consistently strong over the last twenty years, fo'llowing the pattern observed
for the country as a whole; with very rapid growth in the project area at
first, and steadying in the more recent period. Annex 4.1 gives recent
tourism statistics for the project area in relation to the country as a whole,
and Annexes 4.2 and 4.3 show growth in demand and supply in the project area
in recent years. Table 4.1 shows that demand in the project area (tourist
arrivals and overnights) has grown faster than supply, with rates around 5.5%
per year over the 1980-1986 period. Demand has also grown substantially
faster than in Yugoslavia as a whole, while growth in supply has been slower.
Table 4.1: Tourism Annual Growth Rates (1980-1986) a/
(Percent)
Tourist             Tourist           Bed
Arrivals           Overnights       Capacity
Project Area              5.7                 5.4             2.8
Yugoslavia                4.0                 4.6             3.6
a/ At registered accommodations only. Does not include private
homes that take in guests unofficially.



- 12 -
4.04     Decisions regarding the type of facilities to be constructed in the
near future in the project area have already been made and sites cho&ien; for
example, there are plans for, inter alia, 1,600 additional berths for boats in
marinas in Koper and Izola, an additional casino/hotel at Portoroz (in
Slovrenia), as well as three new complexes in Pula, several A-category hotels
and an apartment complex to be built in the Croatian part of the project area.
4.05     However, no new construction of tourist accommodation is being
permitted until the water supply problem has been solved. The lack of an
adequate water supply is the most serious constraint to further expansions of
capacity, as well as the most urgent problem affecting utilization of present
capacity.
The Effect of Water Supply Shortages on Present Earnings and Future Development
4.06     Water supply shortages in 1986 and 1987 had an impact on all the
tourism-related organizations in the project area, and caused problems for
many of the local industries. The most severe water shortages occur during
the dry summer months in the peak of the tourist season. Water rationing was
carried out in various steps, according to the severity of the shortage. At
20% water shortages, hotels are the fourth category in priority to receive
water; higher priority is given to the old towr. centers,l hospitals, and
workers' kitchens. When shortages reach 30X, they are experienced by all
categories in all areas. This was the case in 1987, and interruptions in
supply were long, with some areas receiving water for only a few hours every
other day.  Hotels at higher elevato*ns were particularly hard hit:
insufficient pressure prevented water reaching the guest rooms, even during
the few hours of water service. One of these hotels reported employing
several people to carry water to the rooms, and estimated that there were 12%
fewer overnights in 1987 than in 1986 because of the water shortages.
4.07     All managers in the project area attributed significant losses to
water shortages in both 1986 and 1987. These were estimated to be in the
order of 5-10% of total revenues for the summer of 1987 by two of the large
tourism organizations. Tourist dissatisfaction was widespread and was
manifested in complaints to management, visits being cut short, and in
discounts being negotiated in hotel bills. Campers, who use relatively large
quantities of water, tended to leave after encountering water shortages.
Several managers interviewed felt that some of the decreased demand in 1987
was because of water problems encountered in 1986. There is some worry that
adverse publicity regarding water problems in the project area may affect
tourism in the country as a whole because of returning tourists associating
the problems with Yugoslavia, rather than with the particular area where the
problems occurred.
Other Constraints to Tourism Development
4.08     Inadequate water supply is by far the most pressing constraint to
further tourism development in the region. There are no major inadequacies in
1/ Due to the poor condition of water pipes, inadequate pressure if allowed
could result in contaminated water entering the system.



- 13 -
other infrastructural services. Planned infrastructure investments focus on
improving access to the area. Road improvemsnts in the region will emphasize
upgrading the main roads into the Istrian peninsula in order to increase
access to the interior tourist attractions. However, road traffic does not
constitute a major constraint in the short-term; at most, some improvements in
existing surfaces may be indicated. A cooperative agreement with Italy is
designed to improve sections of major roads north of the Istrian peninsula,
with potential positive effects on the traffic near the border at Trieste.
Any delays encountered in the Italy-Slovenia traffic are more likely to be
attributable to the border check-point than to road conditions.
4.09     Acceesibility by air will be increased when the Pula international
airport improvements are finished. The airport, which currently receives 35%
of the area's 'packaged' tours, will be able to handle wide-bodied jets,
thereby increasing the capacity for this kind of tourism in the area. Sea
access will be improved through several new marinas; these are considered
particularly profitable since marina clients tend to spend more than average
tourists.
Projected Tourism Growth
4.10     Tourist overnights will increase as a result of both the additional
accommodation to be constructed, and of the increased utilization of existing
capacity. Projected growth in overnights averages 3% per year for the total
project area, and 3.0% and 2.9% for Istria and the Slovene Coast, respectively.



- 14 -
V. WATER AND SEWERAGE SERVICE AND THE DEMAND FOR
WATER IN THE PROJECT AREA
Water Supply
5.01    There are six sources owned and operated by the three Water Works.
The largest and most important supply is the Gradole System which is owned
jointly by the three and operated by IWW. The remaining five sources are
owned and operated by the three Water Works and used exclusively for their
respective service areas. The ownership and detailed statistics on the six
sources are listed in Table 5.1. Four of the six sources are naturally
occurring springs which have been enclosed and improved. Spring water is
chlorinated and filtered year round and is chemically coagulated and settled
for four to six weeks out of the year during periods following heavy rains
when the water is turbid. Usually, ground water emerging from springs
requires only chlorination and sometimes treatment for the Iemoval of
dissolved iron or other minerals, but not treatment for turbidity removal.
The karstic geology of the area accounts for the numerous springs and also for
the high turbidity of spring water after heavy rains, because ground water
flows quickly through limestone channels and cavities without being filtered
naturally. Pula's wells are in a non-karstic area and require only
chlorination.

When the yield drops below 1,000 1/sec, which happened in 1987, the
available water is shared in the same proportion as the full capacity
allocations.
5.03       Under the agreement, RWW will be compensated for their share
of the Gradole Supply System based on its original Dinar cost less
depreciation. Inflation is not to be taken into account. RWW has tried
to change the agreement from time to time, first, to allow them to retain
their o-iginal allocation and second, to change the compensation clause
to take inflation into consideration. It is unlikely that they will win
the first point, but since relations between the three Water Works are
good, agreement on the second is possible.
5.04       RWW's Secovlje source is also subject to special agreement.
This source constructed in 1950, is located in Croatia. In order to get
permission to develop it, RWW agreed to stop withdrawals in 1995 and turn
the remaining facilities over to IWW. The Republic of Croatia and iWW
are holding RWW to this agreement.

For the past three years, all three water works have been unable to meet
their peak summertime demand. The result has been customer
inconvenience, lost revenue from tourism and the instigation of costly
water trucking operations. Additional capacity is clearly needed now.
5.06     There are four major trunk main systems bringing water from the
region's sources of supply to its scattered distribution networks. All
four systems distribute water by gravity from a tank to which water has
been pumped at the source. RWW has a single trunk main system serving
networks in each of the three communes it serves. The trunk
system is supplied at one end by the Rizana Spring source and at the
other end by the Gradole and Secovlje sources. IWW has its own trunk
main system supplied from Buzet, connected to two commune networks. The
other four communes in its area are served by the Gradole system. PWW
has only one network, but it also supplies water to the island of Brojni
by a submerged pipeline. Its networks are supplied by the Gradole system
in addition to the two local sources. The Coastal Region terrain is
hilly, requiring numerous small pumping stations and storage tanks.
Table 5.2 gives information on the distribution systems of the three
Water Works. In general, the existing trunk main systems lack sufficient
capacity to deliver the additional water required to meet the growing
demand of the networks and must be augmented. Some of the distribution
networks, especially parts of the Piran network, are old and leak
heavily, and are in need of rehabilitation and replacement.

Sewerage
5.07     Water-borne sewage collection is provided in all developed areas
along the coast and in the community centers. Rural areas and areas
outside the sewerage service areas for the most part are provided for
adequately with septic tanks, cess pits and latrines. The population
provided with water-borne sewage collection is estimated at 65%. New
sewers are designed to carry only sanitary wastes; however, some of the
older sewers are combined and carry sanitary wastes and storm water.
5.08     Treatment and disposal, either in place or proposed for the
coastal corn-unities, consists mainly of mechanical screening, grit
removal and disposal by sea outfall. Koper and Piran have primary
treatment plants, and two tourist complexes in Porec are provided with
secondary plants. The discharge pipes for the Por,c plants are at the
sboreline and the results are not as satisfactory as with systems that
provide a lower level of treatment but discharge through properly
designed outfalls which range in length from 0.5 to 3.0 km. Secondary
treatment is provided for the inland community centers of Buzet and Pazin
and is proposed for Buje center. The Pazin plant serves only 50% of the
population, does not operate satisfactorily, and is too small to serve
the local industries. Most industries provide their own treatment to a
level satisfactory for disposal to the community systems but not for
direct discharge. The few plants that do not treat adequately are
located near Pazin.
5.09     Marine disposal of waste water by sea outfall from community
centers close enough to the coast to make it a practical option has only
been practiced for the past 10 years. Before then, coastal communities
had multiple outlets discharging at or near the shoreline. The design
and construction of proper marine disposal systems began only after beach
conditions deteriorated to the extent that it was noticeable by tourists
and vacationers. The new systems are adequately designed and constructed
and have proved effective. Experts believe that marine organisms can
assimilate untreated domestic wastes that are adequately dispersed in the
marine environment, but not wastes that are given more advanced
treatment. For the systems to work, industrial wastes must be either
kept out of the systems or adequately pretreated to remove toxic
substances. Proper treatment is beIng provided by all industries located
close to the coast; but some still discharge to the nearest water course
or drainage ditch.



- 17 -
5.10     The treatment and disposal of waste water from Pazin in central
Istria and its surrounding industries is the most serious environmental
pollution problem in the project area and has been recognized as such by
both local and Republic of Croatia authorities. Waste water from Pazin
in recent years has been detected in the underground water source for
approximately 50,000 persons in the communities of Pula and Labin. A
solution to the problem has the highest level priority in the Republic's
Social Plan for 1986-1990. The Republican Committee for Water Management
has designated all water community of interest contributions in 1989 for
use in solving the Pazin problem with implementation of the recommended
solution to begin in 1990. A study of the problem was undertaken in 1987
and several alternative solutions recommended, none of which were
completely satisfactory to local interests. A follow-up study to address
the concerns raised in the review of the first study is included in the
Project as well as implementation of the solution (para. 7.14).
Demand for Water
5.11     The demand for water has been calculated separately for the
three Water Works (Annex 5.1). The basis for the calculations are
projections of future permanent population, population served and tourist
overnights. Average annual production requirements were calculated by
multiplying the projected values by estimates of per capita and per
tourist overnight water use based on historical values and adding an
allowance for "unaccounted-for water". Production capacity requirements
were obtained by multiplying average consumption by peaking factors based
on historical values adding unaccounted-for water. The calculation
indicates the following for the year 2005 in comparison to existing
capacities:

5.12.    Population. The permanent service area population remained
relatively stable since the end of the Second World War until 1980.
Since 1980 there has been a moderate increase. The major factor limiting
the growth in permanent population is the availability of housing. More
housing is planned in the service areas of all three Water Works and
planners are projecting average annual growth rates of 1.5% for the area,
mainly as a result of migration. This compares to current annual growth
rates of 0.4% for Slovenia, 0.3% for Croatia and 0.7% for Yugoslavia.

IWW unaccounted-for water would remain at current levels and that the RWW and
PWW action plans would be successful.

VI. THE BORROWERS AND IMPLEMENTING ORGANIZATIONS
Introduction
6.01     Fourteen entities would have financing and/or implementation
responsibilities for the Project. RWW, IWW, and PWW, the three Water Works
responsible for water service in the project area would finance, own, and
operate the water supply components and would be the Bank's Borrowers. RWW
would implement its component. IWW and PWW, along with six benefitting
communities, have joined together to form the Butoniga Water Works (BWW) to
implement their water supply component. Six infrastructure communities of
interest, a regional water community of interest and two community
infrastructure works enterprises would implement the sewage works. The
Committee for Water Management of the Republic of Croatia would carry out a
sewerage master plan study.
The Three Water Works (the Borrowers)
6.02     The Water Works are independent organizations responsible for the
planning, construction and operation of water supply facilities in their
respective service areas. RWW serves the communes of Koper, Izola and Piran
through 22,000 service connections from two exclusively-owned sources and with
water purchased from the Gradole System, which it owns jointly with IWW and
PWW. IWW serves the communities of Buje, Porec, Rovinj, Buzet and Pazin
through 23,000 connections from one exclusively owned source and water from
the jointly-owned Gradole System, which it operates for the benefit of itself
and the other two owners. PWW serves only the community of Pula through
20,000 connections from 12 deep wells and a spring source, which it operates
exclusively for itself, and with water from the Gradole System. The three
Water Works are autonomous except that their investments, annual budgets,
tariffs and borrowings must be approved by the communities they serve. RWW
obtains the community approval through the Association of Coastal Communities
which represents the three communities it serves in matters of joint
interest. IWW must deal with each of the five communities it serves
individually.
6.03     OrganizationandStaffin .  The organizational structure of the three
Water Works are all similar (See Annexes 6.1, 6.2 and 6.3). The highest
decision making authority is vested in the workers' council whose members are
elected for a 2-year term. The principal functions of the council are to:
(i) appoint the managing director, his deputy and department heads; (ii)
approve all changes to the organize'ional structure; and (iii) approve
investment plans, annual budgets and financial statements. Responsibility for
planning and operation of the water works is vested in the managing director
whose term of office is four years and who may be reappointed any number of
times.
6.04     IWW is the largest of the three Water Works with 221 employees.  RWW
has 146 employees and the PWW has 170. The number of employees in each
organization gives the following ratios for employees per 1,000 water
connections:

The IWW ratio is higher than the others since the operates the Gradole water
supply system which serves bulk water to the other Water Works. All the
ratios are satisfactory.
6.05     The top positions in all three organizations are held by experienced,
university trained professionals, most of whom have worked their way up from
junior positions or have transferred from similar positions in other
organizations after having proven themselves to be effective managers or
technicians. Employee turnover is negligible and it is fairly easy to recruit
new employees when needed because of the pleasant living conditions in the
area and shortage of professional type jobs.
6.06     l         is informal at all three Water Works.  All staff are
encouraged to pursue higher education through Water Works grants for books and
tuition; however, since there are few technical schools in the area, the
opportunities for this type of training are limited. Training is also
provided by equipment suppliers for selected staf 2 through training seminars
and on-the-job training whenever new equipment is purchased. This is an area
where the three Water Works cooperate. Recently, for example, all three Water
Works made a joint purchase of leak detection equipment and coordinated the
visit of the supplier's representative to run seminars on leak detection. The
Project would provide for training in leak detection and funds for overseas
training and the purchase of training materials (para. 7.13). Because of the
high level of education of most employees and the low turnover, formal
training programs are not considered necessary.
6.07     Operation and Maintenance. Although all three Water Works have well
maintained facilities and carry out good routine maintenance, the program of
IWW appears to be the best as evidenced by their low unaccounted-for water
(22% as compared to 40% for RWW and 31% for PWW).  IWW has achieved its
success by assigning responsibility, measuring results, and rewarding good
performance. Networks are assigned to specific crews who are responsible for
operation, maintenance, as well as reading of meters. Master meters measure
delivered water to assigned networks so unaccounted-for water by network can
be measured. Crews that maintain low levels of unaccounted-for water in their
assigned networks receive salary bonuses.
6.08     BiliM and CoUection. The sales of all three Water Works are fully
metered, and by the end of 1988, all three will have computerized billing.
PWW currently prepares all its bills by hand but is in the process of
converting to computerized billing. All three water works read meters and
bill monthly and are able to collect over 95% of their billings. RWW has the
best results with a 992 collections record. The receivables for the three
Water Works are all below two months billings, which is satisfactory.



- 22 -
6.09     Insrance. RWW, IWW and PWW carry comprehensive insurance coverage
for fire, floods, natural and other disasters, damage to assets, machinery
failure, staff injuries, public liability, automobiles, inventories, theft,
and construction in progress when not covered by contractors. Insurance
coverage for assets is at replacement cost less depreciation. The insurance
coverage carried by the three Water Works is satisfactory.
The Implementing Agencies
6.10     Twelve independent implementing agencies would carry out the
Project. The Water Supply components would be implemented by RWW, a borrower,
and the Butoniga Water Works (BWW), a special project implementation unit.
The sewerage works would be implemented by six infrastructure communities of
interest, two community infrastructure works enterprises and a regional water
community of interest. The sewerage master plan study would be implemented by
the Committee for Water Management of the Republic of Croatia.
6.11     The Butoniga WaterWorks (BWW). In order to implement the Istria
component of the Project quickly and efficiently, IWW and PWW have formed
BWW. This organization is charged with complete responsibility for the Istria
component of the Project and would be the legal owner until it is turned over
to the two Water Works, at which point BWW will cease to exist. BWW is
charged with not only designing and constructing the works, but also obtaining
the financing. The new organization was felt necessary so that the two Water
Works and six communes would feel equally represented in the effort. A
temporary organization also has certain legal advantages, the most important
being that it has no workers' council which must approve personnel
appointments, budgets and investment plans; and the director and key personnel
have no fixed limit to their terms of office. Difficulties and delays in the
1970s with implementation of the Gradole Water Supply System, a project
jointly owned by the three Water Works, are one reason the new organization
was formed.
6.12     BWW has three levels of authority.  At the highest level is the
Butoniga Committee, consisting of 24 republic and community leaders. The
Committee meets at least once a year and is responsible for establishing
policy. Decisions at the working level, such as the approval of contract
awards and the filling of staff positions, are made by the Butoniga Council.
The Council has 12 members, six Butoniga Water Works staff, and one
representative from each of the six beneficiary communes.  The BWW Director is
a member of both the Committee and the Council.
6.13     At the working level, BWW has three departments whose heads report to
a managing director. The staff will eventually total 16, but only eight
positions have been filled so far. The organization is large for the size of
the Project in comparison to similar organizations for other Bank projects.
The reason for its size is that it is a completely independent organization
with its own personnel and administrative functions and it does not rely on
assistance from the two founding water works. Even so, the staff is probably
larger than necessary. However, the positions are being filled slowly and
only as the need arises, e.g. none of the construction supervision staff has
been hired yet. Most of the staff were previously employed by IWW and PWW and
are well qualified and experienced in the water supply field and in
implementing projects.



- 23 -
6.14     Infrastructure and Water Communities of Interest (ICI). ICIs are formed
in Yugoslavia whenever several groups or organizations have a common problem
that needs to be solved. They can be formed at any level--community, region
or republic. All the communities in the project area have ICIs to arrange
financing for roads, drainage and sewerage. In seven of the communities in
the project area (Koper, Izola, Piran, Buje, Buzet, Rovinj and Pazin) the ICIs
implement the works required in addition to arranging financing. In the other
two, they only arrange financing and the conmunal works organizations
implement the works. The ICIs in six of the communi'ties would implemel't the
sewerage works included in the Project. Because of its regional importance,
the sewerage works in Pazin would be financed and implemented through the
Rijeka Region Water Community of Interest.
6.15     Community Infrastructure Works Enterprises are similar to a community
public works denartment in most member countries of the Bank. All communities
in Yugoslavia have them to operate and maintain the local infrastructure and
provide community services. Some organizations are also responsible for
constructing new works. The communal works organizations in Pula and Porec
will implement the sewerage works included in the Project for their
communities.
6.16     Committee for Water Management of the Republic of Croatia is
responsible for the planning and implementation of water projects of Republic
importance, monitoring compliance with federal and republic discharge
requirements and controlling inter-basin transfers of water among the eight
water regions in Croatia. The Committee also sets priorities for the use of
contributions to the Republic of Croatia Water Community of Interest. At the
policy level, the committee consists of representatives from each of the eight
regions who meet regularly to set priorities and discuss inter-basin issues.
The planning and engineering functions of the committee are carried out by a
staff of 50 located in Zagreb under the direction of the Committee's President.



- 24 -
VII. THE PROJECT
Introduction
7.01     The Project comprises both water supply and sewerage works.  The
water works represent 80% of the planned investments of the three Borrowers
from 1987, when the first project expenditures were made, until project
completion in 1994. The remainder of the Borrowers' planned investments
include trunk main extensions to allow for expanded service, rehabilitation of
existing networks and long range planning. Local networks and service
connections are paid for by the agencies requiring the service as part of the
facilities being constructed; therefore, the costs do not appear in their
investment programs. The sewerage works consist of 21 independent projects to
be implemented and financed by the nine communities in the project area. Bank
loan proceeds would not be used for the sewerage works. They are included in
the Project to ensure that the additional waste water generated as a result of
the Project would be disposed of in an environmentally sound manner.
Project Objectives
7.02     The Project objectives are:  (i) to eliminate existing water
shortages in the project area; (ii) to provide additional water supply
capacity needed for expansion of the tourism industry and growth in domestic
and industrial/commercial demand in the project area; (iii) to reduce
unaccounted-for water in the Rizana and Pula Water Works distribution network;
(iv) to protect existing water sources and coastal tourist areas from
pollution; and (v) to increase efficiency in the delivery of water supply an
sewerage services in the project area.
Project Description
7.03     The Project is divided into the Slovene Coast component and the
Istria component. Each component is described below as a separate project.
The Project parts and their relationship to each other are shown on Map No.
IBRD 20580 attached to this report.
7.04     Slovene Coast Component. This component consists of the following
parts:
Part A: Water Supply and Treatment
1.   Source works to increase the available supply at Rizana from 300
1/sec to 650 1/sec.
2.   Expansion of the Rizana treatment plant from 200 1/sec to 800
1/sec with provision for expansion in the future to 2,600 1/sec.
3.   Planning and investigations for future source development.
Part B: Water Transmission and Storage
1.   5.0 km raw water pipeline from the Rizana source works to the
Rizana water treatment plant.



- 25 -
2.   10.6 km treated water pipeline from the Rizana water treatment
plant to Koper, including modifications to the Pobegi pump
station.
3.   8.0 km treated water pipeline and tunnel from Koper to Izola,
including the St. Mark's Hill pumping station.
4.   Distribution storage totaling 15,000 m3.
5.   A supervisory control and data acquisition (SCADA) system.
Part C: Water Distribution
1.   Rehabilitation and replacement of networks in the old town
centers of Koper, Izola and Piran.
2.   Improvement of pumping station control systems.
3.   Establishment of waste control districts.
Part D: Institutional Strengthening of the Rizana Water Works
1.   Technical assistance for the reduction of unaccounted-for water.
2.   A study of the Borrower's tariff structure.
3.   Operation and maintenance equipment, incldding instrumentation
for water loss control.
4.  Training courses and materials for the training the staff of RWW.
Part E: Sewerage
1.   In the Community of Piran, a pumping station and forcemain for
connecting the old town of Piran sewerage network to the Piran
Sewage Treatment Plant.
2.   In the Community of Izola:  (a) a 2.0 km relief sewer in the old
town of Izola connecting the new town area with the main sewage
pumping station; and (b) rehabilitation and expansion to 270
1/sec of the main sewage pumping station in the old town of
Izola.
3.   In the Community of Koper:  (a) upgrading and expansion of the
Koper Sewage Treatment Plant from a preliminary treatment plant
to a primary treatment plant with a population equivalent
capacity of 100,000; (b) a 0.5 km sea outfall for the disposal
of treated sewage from the Koper sewage treatment plant; and (c)
a 2.0 km collector sewer from the Iplas and Lama factories to
the main sewerage network.
7.05     The works in Parts A and B of the component are the first phase of a
two phase plan to expand the Rizana River supply system to its potential



- 26 -
average safe yield of 1,600 1/sec (maximum day capacity of 2,400 1/sec).
Until 1987, the Rizana source consisted of an enclosed spring with safe yield
of 200 1/sec. In 1987, the Water Works constructed two deep wells with a
combined pumping capacity of 350 1/sec which tap the same aquifer as the
spring. The pumps are oaily used when the flow from the spring drops below the
required system demand. Three additional wells with a combined capacity of
450 1/sec have already been drilled and tested. Under Part Al pumps would be
instailed in these wells and a collection system constructed to convey well
water to an existing central raw water pumping station. In the future, a
storage reservoir would be constructed to store the surface water flow of the
Rizana River. The reservoir would increase the safe yield of the system to
800 1/sec which could support a maximum day capacity of 1200 1/sec.
Eventually, the Rizana system would be combined with the system of a
neighboring water works, and a second surface water reservoir would be
constructed for the combined service area of the two systems (see Map).  The
treatment plant expansion under Part A2 would consist of the addition of rapid
sand filters, mixing and settling tanks and the addition of more treated water
storage to the existing treatmei.t plant.  The existing treatment process is
satisfactory and would not be changed under the Project. Part A3 would
consist of drilling and testing for the construction of new wells in the
Rizana drainage area.
7.06     The transmission system under Part B of the component would transmit
the raw water from the source works to the treatment plant and from the
treatment plant to the areas of greatest demand in the water works service
area. The transmission main is sized for the saturation demand expected in
2015. Constructing the transmission main in a single stage was determined to
be the least-cost solution. The distribution storage is being staged with
only 50% of the ultimate reqiirement being constructed under the Project. The
first half of the transmission main in Part B2 was completed and put in
service in June 1988. The second half is scheduled for completion by June
1989. The Bank will not participate in the financing of these parts of the
Project.
7.07     Part C of the component would reduce water wastage and improve the
efficiency of distribution system operations. Most of the system's leakage
occurs in the networks of the three old town centers of Koper, Izola and
Piran. RWW currently has a program for replacing the older pipes in these
networks. Under the Project, this effort would continue but at an accelerated
rate so that by the end of the Project all the old pipe would be replaced.
The network replacement in the old town centers is a part of RWW's overall
waste control program.  Another part is the establishment of the waste control
districts in Part C2. Part C3 would reduce the number of personnel required
to operate the system's 36 pumping stations.
7.08     Part D of the component would provide consultants, equipment and
training for the institutional improvement of RWW. Consultants would provide
technical assistance for the reduction of unaccounted-for water (para. 5.17)
and for carrying out a tariff study (para. 8.05) under Parts Dl and D2. Part
D3 would make available instruments and special equipment not available
locally for the reduction of unaccounted-for water and genera: maintenance of
the water system. The training in Part D4 is designed to allow water works
personnel to attend training courses and seminars outside Yugoslavia and to
obtain foreign training materials.



- 27 -
7.09     Parts El, E2 and E3 of the component are sewerage works that would be
carried out by the three communities in the project area to provide the
capacity necessary to adequately collect, treat and dispose of the additional
waste water resulting from the Project. All the works are included in the
five year plans for the three communities and are justified on the basis of
adequately prepared master plans.
7.10      stria Component. This component consists of the following parts:
Part A. Water SuplAy and Treatment
1.  Sanitary protective zone for t.e Butoniga Reservoir.
2. The Butoniga water treatment plant with a capacity of 1,000
1/sec, expandable in the future to 2,000 l/sec, including a raw
water pumping station and a treated water pumping station.
Part B. Transmission and Storage
1. 12.0 km of raw water and treated water pipeline from the Butoniga
rr,servoir to the Butoniga treatment plant (0.8 kim) and from the
Butoniga treatment plant to Beram (11.2 km).
2. 46.0 km treated water pipeline from Beram to Loborika with 32.7
km of branching trunk mains connecting the community centers of
Rovinj, Pazin and Pula, and a pumping station at Pazin.
3. Distribution storage totaling 19,000 m3.
4. Telemetering and controls for system operation.
Part C. Institutional Strengthening of the Butoniga, Istrian and
Pula Water Works
1. Technical assistance for the reduction of unaccounted-for water
in the Pula Water distribution system.
2. A study of the feasibility of combining the Istrian and Pula
Water Works and the operation of the Butoniga water supply system.
3. A study of the Borrower's tariff structures.
4. A sewerage master plan for Central Istria.
5. Equipment for the implemnentation, operation and maintenance of
the Butoniga water supply system and instrumentation for water
loss control in the Pula water distribution system.
6. Training courses and materials for the training of the staffs of
IWW, PWW and BWW.
Part D. Sewerage
1. In the Community of Pula: (a) the Valbandor sewerage system
consisting of the Valbandor-Fazana collector, the Valbandor
sewerage network, and two pumping stations; (b) the



- 28 -
Verudele/Pjescana uvala sewerage system and the Pula town
interceptor connecting to the Trafo stanica pumping station; (c)
completion of the Banjole sewage system, including 4.0 km of
collector; and (d) the Medulin sewerage system consisting of a
0.7 km sea outfall, 220 1/sec sewage treatment plant, 7.0 km of
sewers and 3 pumping stations.
2. In the Community of Rovinj: (a) the Rovinj center 2.3 km
interceptor, including three pumping stations (Skver, Valbanolon,
and Kino) and a 0.3 km forcemain; and (b) the Polari pumping
station and the 2.0 km Polari-Kuvi collector.
3. In the Community of Porec, an interceptor and three pumping
stations connecting the existing sewage treatment plants at
Cervar, Lanterna and Ulika with the existing sewage treatment
plant at Materada and the extension of the Materada sea outfall
from 1.5 km to 2.0 km.
4. In the Community of Buje: (a) a secondary treatment plant for
the old town of Buje to serve an equivalent population of 7,000;
(b) a preliminary sewage treatment plant and 1.5 km sea outfall
for the old town of Novigrad to serve an equivalent population of
10,000; (c) a preliminary sewage treatment plant and the
extension of a 0.3 km outfall to 1.5 km for Savudreja to serve an
equivalent population of 15,000.
5. In the Community of Buzet, sewage networks in Buzet center to
extend service to an existing unserved population of 800 (20% of
total).
6. In the Community of Pazin, a sewage treatment and disposal system
for the old town of Pazin and the nearby industries.
7.11     The works in Parts A and B of the component, known locally as the
Butoniga water supply system, would withdraw water from the existing Butoniga
reservoir, treat it in a conventional rapid sand treatment plant, and pump it
to a distribution reservoir on a hill near the treatment plant. From the
treatment plant, water would flow through an 87 km transmission system to the
distribution networks of IWW and PWW. The transmission system consists of
steel pipe ranging in diameter from 1,200 mm to 250 mm, three large
distribution reservoirs, four smaller break-pressure tanks and one booster
pumping station. The treatment plant and transmission system is designed for
and ultimate capacity of 2,000 1/sec. The Bank Project consists of the first
stage construction, with the treatment plant, distribution reservoirs and
in-line pumping station sized for 1,000 I/sec. The pipeline is sized for the
ultimate capacity which is the least-cost solution. The capacity of the
system has been allocated between the two Water Works as follows: IWW - 57X;
PWW - 43a.
7.12     The Butoniga Reservoir was originally designed as a flood control and
irrigation reservoir by the Republican Committee for Water Management, and was
constructed with pooled funds from water communities of interest (WCI)



- 29 -
throughout Croatia. Construction began in 1985 aid was completed in 1987.
When water shortages first occurred in 1983, IWW and PWW obtained the
approval of the Republican Comnittee for Water Management to use the
irrigation storage capacity for domestic and industrial water supply. The
reservoir at present belongs to the Republic of Croatia; however, IWW has
assumed responsibility for the operation and maintenance of the reservoir and
has requested the Republic to transfer ownership to them. Legal ownership is
not a requirement for its use nor is it considered an issue. The cost of the
reservoir, including the intake tower in current prices was $12.0 million
equivalent. La.ad acquisition for a protective zone around the reservoir will
cost an estimated $0.6 million equivalent in December 1987 prices. This cost
is included in the project cost; the construction cost of the dam and
reservoir is not.
7.13     Part C of the component would provide consultants, equipment and
training for the institutional improvement of BWW, IWW and PWW.  Consultants
would provide technical assistance for the reduction of unaccounted-for water
in the PWW water distribution network (para. 5.17), tariff studies for IWW and
PWW (paras. 8.13 and 8.21) and a water management study (para. 7.31) under
Parts Cl, C2 and C3. Part C4, the sewerage master plan, is discussed in para.
7.14 below. Parts C5 and C6 would provide equipment and training to assist in
the implementation of the Butoniga water supply system and its operation upon
completion.  The training in Part C6 is designed to allow BWW, IWW and PWW
personnel to attend training courses and seminars outside Yugoslavia and to
obtain foreign training materials.
7.14     The sewerage works in Parts Dl through D6 of the component would be
carried out by the six communities in the Istria component project area and
have the same objective as the sewerage works in the Slovene Coast component.
Parts Dl through D6 are well defined by feasibility studies, and in some cases
final designs, and are in the respective community five-year plans. When
complete, they will meet the area's projected sewage collection, treatment and
disposal needs until the year 2000 with the exception of Pazin. Only Part D6
a sewage treatment and disposal system for the old town of Pazin, requires
further study and definition. The proposal is for a secondary treatment plant
based on the assumption that the treated effluent would be disposed of in the
Lim River. Tourist enterprises in Istria have objected to the disposal of any
sewage effluent in the Lim River. Consequently, further study is needed,
which would be undertaken as part of a master plan study of waste water
disposal in Central Istria, Part C4. The four coastal communities already
have adequate master plans which are being implemented and additional studies
are not required. Outline terms of reference for the study agreed in
negotiations are included in Annex 7.1.
Project Cost
7.15     The combined cost of the two components including the sewerage works
is $244 million; $103 million for the Slovene Coast component and $14i million
for the Istria component. Allowances for customs duties of $3.1 million for
imported equipment and materials is included.  Enginee.ing costs include only
the cost of consultants. The in-house costs of water works and community
works organization personnel administering the Project are not included.
These costs are reflected in the financial statements of the Borrowers.



- 30 -
Consulting services for design, construction supervision, technical assistance
and studies are estimated at $15.8 million ($14.8 million in base costs).
These costs do not include the cost of design and engineering work completed
prior to 1987. Man-month requirements for the consulting services are
estimated at 5,000 man-months, of which 20 man-months would be foreign
consultants engaged for technical assistance and studies. It is probable that
the remaining design work and construction supervision will be carried out by
Yugoslav engineering organizations already engaged in preparing the final
designs, since the officials responsible for implementing the Project are
satisfied with the engineering work already completed. These consultants are
satisfactory to the Bank, and there is no reason why they should not be
retained to finish the work they have started.
7.16     The project cost estimates for the two components are summarized in
Tables 7.1 and 7.2. Base costs are in December 31, 1988 prices. The grand
total for both components of the Project include physical contingencies of
$18.9 million (10% of base osts for equipment, materials, and civil works)
and price contingencies of $16.9 million (8% of the dollar base cost plus
physical contingencies; the assumed annual rates of inflation of dollar prices
are 3.0% for 1989, 3.5% for 1990, and 4.0% thereafter). The estimated foreign
exchange requirement is $82.7 million (35% of the total cost). The bases for
the cost estimate and further breakdowns of costs are presented in Annex
7.2. More detailed figures are in the Project File.
Bank Lending Arrangements
..17     The Bank would lend $60 million equivalent--$32 million to RWW for
the water works (Parts A, B, C and D) in the Slovene Coast component and $28
million to IWW and PWW for the water works (Parts A, B and C) in the Istria
component. IWW and PWW would be jointly and severally responsible for their
loan. The loan to RWW would finance an estimated 31% of the Slovene Coast
component.  The loan to IWW and PWW would finance an estimated 20% of the
Istria component including $3.9 million in interest and other charges accruing
on the Bank loan during construction. Justification for the financing of
interest is based on the need for IWW and PWW to improve their cash
requirements during project implementation.
Availability of Foreign Exchange for Loan Repayment and Assumption of the Foreign
Exchange Risk
7.18     RWW, IWW and PWw are not foreign exchange earners; therefore, under
the foreign currency relations law they must make special arrangements in
order to borrow from the Bank. The options under the law are as follows: (a)
they can be granted an exception through special federal legislation, or (b)
they can obtain a commitment of a foreign exchange earning enterprise to make
the foreign exchange available to them when it is needed for repayment of the
Bank loan. The Water Works would use the latter approach, with the tourist
enterprises in their respective service areas providing the foreign exchange.
The Water Works would assume the foreign exchange risk and would purchase the
foreign exchange from these enterprises at the official exchange rate. The
Water Works have submitted documentation confirming the agreement of
organizations who would make foreign exchange available for loan repayment.

Counterpart Financing
7.19   The coutnterpart requirements, estimated at $184 million equivalent,
would be financed from charges to customers, tourist taxes and contributions
from local and republic WCIs. Counterpart funds for the water works, Parts A,
B, C and D in the Slovene Coast component and Parts A, B and C in the Istria
component are fully committed and the commitments documented. Commitments are
based on a percentage of project cost which assures their availability, even
in the existing environment of high inflation. The financing of the sewerage
works, Part E of the Slovene Coast component and Part D of the Istria
component involves nine different financing plans, one for each of the
communities in the project area. At appraisal, the sources of financing were
identified but not fully committed. About 40% of the financing for both the
water works and the sewerage works would come from tariffs charged during
project implementation. The remainder of the financing would come from
contributions from er.terprises, contributions from republic WCIs and tourist
taxes.

tables 7.3 and 7.4.
