Proiect Implementation
7.21     Each component of the Project would be implemented independently of
the other and in accordance with the schedules in Annexes 7.3 and 7.4. There
are ten implementing agencies other than RWW, IWW and PWW who would be
responsible for implementing parts of the Project. The responsibilities of
these agencies would be detailed in ten separate agreements between them and
the Water Works. IWW and PWW already have a signed agreement with BWW
satisfactory to the Bank. RWW, IWW and PWW agreed in negotiations to enter
into agreements satisfactory to the Bank with the nine implementing entities



- 35 -
for the sewerage works as a condition of loan effectiveness. The completion
date for the Project would be December 31, 1994.
7.22     Slovene Coast Component.  The Slovene Coast component would be
implemented by four organizations with RWW, the Borrower for the component,
being responsible for overall project coordination. The water works, Parts A,
B, C and D of the component, would be implemented by RWW's Department of
Research and Investment. The sewerage works, Parts El, E2 and E3 of the
component, would be implemented by infrastructure communities of interest in
the three benefitting communities (Koper, Izola and Piran). All four
implementing organizations have engaged, or plan to engage, consultants or
engineering organizations for detailed design, construction supervision,
inspection and studies. RWW has already appointed a project coordinator,
satisfactory to the Bank, to oversee the work of the four implementing
organizations and to see that all Bank requirements are met.
7.23     The detailed engineering and construction supervision would most
likely be carried out by Yugoslav organizations. Part Dl, unaccounted-for
water control technical assistance, would be the only engineering work
requiring foreign firms. Engineers for Parts A and B of the component have
already been selected. They are the Water Resources Institute of Ljubljana
for design, ZRMK Consulting of Ljubljana for the inspection of pipe
installation, and Investbiro in Koper for the inspection of civil works. All
three firms have prior involvement in the Project, and RWW is satisfied with
their work. These organizations are acceptable to the Bank. RWW would do its
own engineering for Part Cl and C3 of the component (distribution system
rehabilitation and establishment of waste districts), but would hire
engineering services for Part C2 (pump station controls), most likely from a
Yugoslav organization. The communities of Koper, Izola and Piran plan to use
engineering organizations that they have worked with in the past to assist
them with implementation of the sewerage works in Part E of the Project.
ThIese organizations are also satisfactory to the Bank.
7.24     Istria Component.  The Istria component would be implemented by
eight organizations with overall coordination provided by BWW which would also
implement the water works, Parts A, B and C (except C4) of the component. The
sewerage works, Parts Dl through D6, would be implemented by: the
infrastructure communities of interest of the communities of Buzet, Rovinj and
Buje; the community infrastructure works enterprises of Pula and Porec; and
the Rijeka Region Water Community of Interest (WCI). The Rijeka Region WCI
would implement the sswerage works in Pazin (para. 6.14). IWW and PWW agreed
in negotiations to carry out through the Committee for Water Management of the
Republic of Croatia the Central Istria Master Plan Sewerage Study, Part C4, in
accordance with terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank no later than
December 31, 1990. BWW has already appointed an individual satisfactory to
the Bank to be the project coordinator. All the implementing organizations
have engaged or plan to engage consultants or engineering organizations for
detailed design, construction supervision, inspection and studies.
7.25     The Consultants and engineering organizations who will assist in the
implementation of the component would most likely be Yugoslav. The
engineering and final design for the water works, Parts A and B of the
component, was carried out by Energoprojekt of Belgrade, a large Yugoslav
engineering organization with a reputation for good work locally as well as
internationally. BWW plans to use them to supervise the construction. The



- 36 -
Ruder Institute of Zagreb, who inspected the manufacture of the pipe that has
already been purchased and delivered, would inspect the installation of the
pipe. Both firms have performed well on the work accomplished to date and are
satisfactory to the Bank. The engineering for the sewerage works (Part Dl
through D6 of the component) would be carried out by Yugoslav organizations
that have worked for the communities in the past. These organizations are
also satisfactory to the Bank. The Committee for Water Management of the
Republic of Croatia would most likely engage the Rijeka Planning Institute to
carry out the Sewerage Study (Part C4) since the Institute did a satisfactory
job with the initial study undertaken in 1987.
Status of Preparation
7.26     The status of preparation for the procurement cf works, govds and
studies to be carried out under the Project is well advanced. Final designs
and terms of reference have been prepared for 69% of the value of these items
for the total Project. Final designs are available for 56X of the value of
the sewerage works. Procurement, including procurement for sewerage works,
would be carried out through 90 contracts: 49 for the Slovene Coast component
and 41 for the Istria component. The status of each of these contracts is in
Annexes 7.6 and 7.7. The status of procurement preparation as of March 22,
1989, in terms of number of contracts and value of procurement, is as follows:

7.27     Land acquisition is well advanced and is not expected to cause delays
in project implementation. Land for the water treatment plants for both
Project components and for the source works under the Slovene Coast component
has already been acquired. An estimated 2,200 easements are required for the
transmission mains--200 for the Slovene Coast component and 2,000 for the
Istria component. About 80X of these have been obtained. Land for the
distribution tanks still must be obtained. About 70X of the land required for
the sewerage works is already owned. Legal means are available to all
implementing organizations if agreement with property owners can not be
reached through negotiation. Land for all the sewage plants has been obtained.
Interim Measures to Limit Water Shortages
7.28     A major focus of the Bank's supervision will be to ensure that
existing resources are managed so that water shortages before completion of
the Project are minimized and that the phasing of the Project is such that the



- 37 -
completed works can be put into use as soon as possible. Both RWW and BWW
have undertaken measures to reduce water shortages in the interim. These
measures include early construction of project parts that can be immediately
ucilized upon completion and the construction of temporary works. So far,
measures by both Water Works have proved successful. Water shortages during
the 1988 summer season occurred only in the PWW service area and were not
nearly as severe as during the summer of 1987.
7.29     RWW completed the first 5.6 km of the Rizana-Koper transmission main
in June 1988 and has under construction of the remaining 5.6 km. Completion
of this section of transmission main will eliminate the most critical capacity
restriction in the RWW distribution system and allow utilization of recently
developed capacity at the Rizana source. This added capacity should eliminate
summertime shortages until the Slovene Coast component is completed in 1994.
7.30     BWW has undertaken two measures to ensure an adequate raw water
supply for the Gradole water supply system. The Gradole spring, which until
July 1988 was the only raw water source for the Gradole water treatment plant,
during the height of the tourist season in 1987 yielded only 50% of its rated
safe yield and the design capacity of the system. As an emergency measure to
ensure a raw water supply for the Gradole system, BWW has constructed a
pipeline and pumping system to deliver 80 1/sec from a previously untapped
spring at Cize and constructed a temporary pumping station at the Butoniga
reservGir. It has constructed the Butoniga-Beram section of the Butoniga
transmission main (Part B2 of the Istria component) to pump reservoir water
into a sink hole which studies show will augment flows from the Gradole spring
20 km distant. The capacity of the diversion scheme is 400 1/sec. These two
measures are designed to prevent a recurrenice of the shortages which occurred
in the summer of 1987.
Operation of the Completed Works
7.31     The Slovene Coast water component would be operated by RWW and the
sewerage works would be operated by the beneficiary communities; however, it
is not clear who would operate the Butoniga water supply system. A number of
alternatives have been suggested. These include: operation by IWW under an
arrangement similar to the one for the Gradole water supply system; operation
by BWW; privatization; and the combining of IWW and PWW into a new regional
system which would operate all systems in the project area including the
Butoniga system. These alternatives would be studied under Part C2 of the
Project. IWW and PWW agreed in negotiations, not later than December 31,
1992, to carry out the study on the basis of terms of reference satisfactory
to the Bank, consult the Bank before March 31, 1993 on its findings and
recommendations and promptly thereafter take measures necessary to obtain the
approval of the agreed recommendations by the appropriate authorities.
Outline terms of reference for the study are in Annex 7.8.
Procurement

valued at $146 million equivalent would be carried out in accordance with
World Bank Procurement Guidelines. The remaining $94 million equivalent of
project costs is reserved procurement and land. Reserved procurement consists

procurement will not affect the Project adversely. The Bank loan would only
be used to finance procurement carried out in accordance with its Procurement

Istrian component during its grace period. Of the procurement tha would be

remaining 8% of the goods would be low cost items of operation and maintenance
equipment and specialty items needed to complete work already in progress
(contracts under $200,000) which would be procured through international and
local shopping. The remaining 21% of the works would be for the construction
of the distribution reservoirs and short sections of pipeline (contracts
smaller than $1,500,000) which would be procured using Local Competitive

7.33     Approval of US$7.0 million equivalent of advance contracting (4.8% of
procurement following Bank Guidelines) of which the Bank would retroactively
finance US$2.0 million equivalent (3.3% of the loan amounts) (para. 7.42) is
recommended for civil works contracts for pipe installation under Part B2 of
the Slovene Coast component and for pipe installation and the construction of
a water tank under Parts Bl and B3 of the Istria component. The contracts
were awarded after June 1, 1988 using LCB procedures satisfactory to the
Bank. The largest contract is less than the US$1.5 million equivalent
contract limit agreed to in negotiations for use of LCB for awarding civil
works contracts. Justification is based on the fact that the works in
question can be put into service immediately and are an important part of the
Borrower's plan to manage water shortages before completion of the Project
(para. 9.06). Therefore, they could not be delayed until after loan signing.
7.34     An estimated $38 million equivalent in contracts (26% of the
Bank-financed procurement) is expected to be won by foreign suppliers and
consultants (See Annexes 7.6 and 7.7). Goods contracts for the supply of
water treatment plant equipment, large pumping equipment and most of the
operation and maintenance equipment are likely to be awarded to foreign
suppliers since these are items not manufactured in Yugoslavia. Yugoslav
suppliers, who would be competitive, are likely to be awarded contracts for
goods manufactured locally, such as steel pipe. Qualifying local
manufacturers would receive a preference in bid evaluation of 15% of the CIF
price or the prevailing import duty, whichever is less. All of the civil
works contracts are expected to be won by local contractors who are qualified
and difficult to underbid in Yugoslavia. Of the consulting assignments, only
the technical assistance for the control of unaccounted-for water for RWW and
PWW is expected to be awarded to a foreign consultant. Local engineering
organizations already involved in the preliminary design and construction
already completed or in progress are likely to be awarded the design and
construction supervision work still remaining.
7.35     Draft tender documents and proposed awards for goods having an
estimated contract value of more than $200,000 equivalent and for civil works
having an estimated value of more than $500,000 equivalent would be subject to
prior review by the Bank. Contracts for lesser amounts would be subject to
ex-post review. These limits would result in prior review of contracts
representing about 801 of all procurement to be financed by the proposed loans.
Environmental Inpact
7.36     The sewerage works in the Project would ensure that the overall
environmental impact of the Project will be positive. By increasing the water
1/ LCB procedures in Yugoslavia are generally consistent with the need for
economy and efficiency in the execution of the proposed Project. There
are, however, a few procedures which are inconsistent with the Procurement
Guidelines of the World Bank, and there are others which require
clarification. The Government and Borrowers agreed in negotiations on the
changes needed to make the procedures acceptable to the Bank.



- 40 -
supply to the project area, the Project would increase the volume of sewage
flows discharged to the Adriatic. Minimum increase is expected inland since
it is only in coastal areas that the water demand is increasing. Marine
disposal and natural treatment by mixing and diffusion, the current practice
in the project area, is adequate according to experts. An estimated 70% of
the wastewater is currently being disposed of by this method through
adequately designed sea outfalls. Included in the Project are works that
would provide proper treatment and disposal for all sewage now being disposed
of improperly and a study that would provide a master sewage disposal plan for
Central Istria where none now exists. The most serious pollution problem in
the project area, the disposal of domestic and industrial sewage from Pazin
(para. 5.08), would be solved.
Disbursements
7.37     Disbursements from the proposed loans would be made for:
(a)  100% of foreign expenditures and 30% of local expenditures for
civil works;
(b) 100% of foreign expenditures, 1002 of local expenditures
(ex-factory cost) and 85 X of local expenditures (off-the-self)
for goods;
(c) 100% of foreign expenditures and 50% of local expenditures for
consultants, training and project related travel of the
Borrower's personnel; and
(d) interest during construction (loan for Istria component only).
7.38     Withdrawal applications would be supported by standard documentation
with the exception of applications for expenditures against contracts valued
at less that $200,000 equivalent, which would be submitted on the basis of
Statements of Expenditures (SOE). Use of the SOE procedure is justified based
on the appraisal mission's evaluation of two implementing organizations'
accounting and administrative procedures, which were found to be
satisfactory.
7.39     Special Accounts would be opened, one for the Slovene Coast component
of the Project at the Ljubjanska Bank and one for the Istria component of the
Project at the Rijeka Bank. The initial deposits for both accounts would be
$3.0 million from the proposed loans which is equivalent to four months of
disbursements during the period with the highest rate of loan drawdown. The
Borrowers would make withdrawals from the respective accounts for the Bank's
share of their components based upon appropriate documentation. IWW and PWW
have authorized BWW to make withdrawals in their behalf. When disbursements
from either account would total one-third the initial deposit, the
organization responsible would apply to the Bank for replenishment of their
Account.
7.40     RWVI, IWW and PWW agreed in negotiations that they would cause their
SOE and Special Accounts to be audited annually by the SDK. The report on the
audit for the Slovene Coast component would be submitted to the Bank by RWW at



- 41 -
the time of submission of the audit report of their financial statements
(para. 8.27). The report on the audit for the Istria component would be
submitted to the Bank by BWW after being approved by IWW and PWW. Both
reports would be due eight months following the close of the fiscal year.
7.41     The proposed allocation of loan proceeds by disbursement category for
each of the two loans is shown in Annex 7.9 for the Slovene Coast component
and Annex 7.10 for the Istria component. The disbursement period for each
would be six years, which is the same as the disbursement period for
investment-type loans for all sectors in Yugoslavia. A schedule of expected
disbursements by quarter for each loan is presented in Annex 7.11 for the
Slovene Coast component and Annex 7.12 for the Istria component.
7.42     Retroactive financing up to US$2.0 million (equivalent US$1.0 million
for each loan) is recommended for expenditures incurred on contracts signed
after June 1, 1988 (para. 7.33). The closing date for each loan would be
December 31, 1995.
Monitorinj and Evaluation
7.43     RWW, IWW and PWW agreed in negotiations to submit to the Bank
semiannual reports (by March 15 and September 15) on the progress of project
implementation during the preceding semesters ending December 31 and June 30,
respectively. BWW would submit the report on the Istrian component on behalf
of IWW and PWW. The reports would measure performance against the
construction and procurement schedules (Annexes 7.3 and 7.4) and the
monitoring indicatcrs (Annex 7.5), and they would include progress reports on
the sewerage works being implemented by others. The agreemenns between the
Water Works and the implementing agencies for the sewerage works (para. 7.21)
would include reporting requirements. The report would be submitted together
with the report on the finances of the Water Works (para. 8.27). The three
Water Works also agreed to submit to the Bank a project completion report on
each component six months after the closing dates for the two loans.

VI. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
Ikttroduction
8.01     This section reviews the past financial performance of the three
Borrowers and presents projections of their future performance and their
financial capacity for carrying out their proposed investment programs,
including the Bank Project. Historical and projected financial statements
have been reclassified and are presented in accordance with international
accounting standards. However, for definitions of some items which remain in
accord±.ice with the Yugoslav accounting practice, see Annex 8.13. The
detailed financial statements, upon which the review is based, are included in
Annexes 8.1 to 8.12.
8.02     Historical and future costs are presented on different bases as a
result of the high inflation rates being experienced in Yugoslavia at the
present time (170% in 1988). Historical data presented in this section and in
the Annexes are in current Dinars. All projection costs are based on the
prevailing prices in December 1987. For purposes of consistency, the proposed
project costs and the related Bank loan and debt service are included in the
projected financial statements at base cost (including physical contingencies
but excluding price contingencies).
Rizana Water Works (RWW)
8.03     Past Performance and Present Position. The actual and projected
Investment Program and Financing Plan, Inco¢i Statements, Balance Sheets, and
Statements of Source and Application of Funds, from 1983 through 1996, are set
forth in Annexes 8.1, 8.2, 8.3 and 8.4, respectively. Explanations of the
accounts reflected in the financial statements and assumptions for the
projections from 1988 to 1996 are provided in Annex 8.14.
8.04     During the period 1983 to 1985, the operating ratio averaged about
931. This high ratio was attributable to tariffs not being increased
sufficiently to offset the impact of inflation on operating costs. Water
tariffs, however, were increased sufficiently in 1986 and 1987 to compensate
for inflation of the prior years and resulted in achieving a reduction of the
operating ratio to a level of about 73% for 1987. Although net income after
taxes was very low for the years 1983, 1984, and 1985 (3, 11, and 14 Dinar
million, respectively), the financial position and self-financing of capital
expenditures of RWW was satisfactory for these years and for the ensuing years
1986 and 1987, as indicated in the balance sheet (Annex 8.3), and statement of
source and application of funds (Annex 8.4). For the year 1987 the ratio of
debt to debt plus equity was 2X, and the debt service was covered 252 times by
internal funds generation due to the low level of past borrowing, and the
current ratio was 2.0. Accounts receivable, however, amounted to about 4.9
months of average 1987 sales. This was attributable to a substantial increase
in tariffs in NovemDer and December of 1987 which were reflected in the
year-end balance of receivables and not in the average year's sales used for
calculating this indicator. Accounts receivable adjusted to. average 1987
tariffs would represent about 1.5 months average sales which is satisfactory.
The self-financing ratio of capital expenditures, including customer
(community) contributions, was 87.71 (Annex 8.4).



- 43 -
8.05     Water Tariffs. RWW tariffs in effect at appraisal are shown in Table
8.1. All customers are charged for a minimum quantity of 5 m3 per month.
The three communities served by RWW are charged uniform tariffs which are
calculated to cover O&M costs, payments to workers, depreciation, and a
contribution to new investments. Changes in tariffs must be approved by the
Workers' Council at the Water Works and the Executive Council of the
Association of Coastal Communities. Tariffs are frequently increased to
compensate for the impact of inflation on operating costs. The tariff
structure does not sufficiently reflect the scarcity of water during the peak
summer months nor the fact that tourism is responsible for most of the new
investment in capacity. RWW agreed in negotiations, not later than December
31, 1990, to undertake a study of its tariff structure on the basis of terms
of reference satisfactory to the Bank; to consult with the Bank on the
findings and recommendations before March 31, 1991; and promptly thereafter to
take measures to put into effect a revised tariff structure. The study,
amongst other considerations, would address the following:
(i)  seasonal surcharge on industrial/commercial, and residential
customer tariffs during the period of annual peak-demand in
order to suppress the level of demand during this period; and
(ii)  separate tariff for establishments providing tourist
accommodation and services.
Outline terms of reference for the tariff study are provided in Annex 8.15.
Table 8.1: RWW Tariffs (EfGective March 31, 1989)
Customer Category
ind./Com.     Residential
Basic Charge for 5 m3 (Din/month)             72,100          36,050
Use Charge for 6 m3 & more (Din/m3)           14,400           7,200
8.06      Future Finncia Performance. RWW's projected financial performance
(Annex 8.2) is satisfactory. The operating ratio is projected to decrease
from about 73% in 1987 to about 63% in 1992, and subsequently to return to
about the 1987 level by 1996 which is projected to be 72%. The 1472 increase
in average revenue per cubic meter of sales shown for 1988 is in line with
inflation during 1987; the size of this increase reflects the large difference
between December 1987 prices (used thereafter as the base price for the
projections), and the average price for 1987. Average revenue per cubic meter
of sales, in constant prices, is subsequently expected to increase by about
48% in 1989 and by 402 in 1991. Thereafter, to 1996, average revenue per cm
is projected to remain relatively unchanged. The rate of return on net
revalued fixed assets is projected to increase from 18.3% in 1987 to 26.62 in
1991, and thereafter decrease to 10.3% in 1996. This is attributable to the



- 44 -
increasing fixed assets base, and to the increasing depreciation, debt
interest, and relatively constant tariffs, from 1992 to 1996. Debt service
(Annex 8.4) is projected to be covered by internal resources by ratios ranging
from about 25:1 to 4:1 during the forecast period 1988 to 1996, and is
satisfactory. The financing of capital expenditure from internal resources
(including customer contributions) is satisfactory for the 1988 to 1996
projection period, as indicated by the self-financing ratios (Annex 8.4).
This indicator increases from about 88% in 1987 to 90% in 1994, and
subsequently to over 1002 in 1996. The excess over 1002 in the years 19&),
1991, and 1995-96 represents the relative amount available for an increase in
the investment program during the 1989-96 period, and for the ensuing years,
with no necessity for reliance for financing from external sources or customer
contributions.
8.07      RWW's indicators reflecting the financial position of the
organization over the 1988-96 forecast period, as presented at the foot of the
balance sheet (Annex 8.3), are satisfactory and demonstrate that RWW' will
maintain a healthy financial position. The ratio of debt to debt plus equity
is projected to be in a range of about 12 to 24, the current ratio in a range
of about 6 to 18X, and the number of months of average sales reflected in the
accounts receivable is projected to decrease from about 4.8 in 1987 to under
one month's average sales from 1991 to 1996.
8.08      RWW agreed in negotiations to maintain tariffs at a sufficient level
to produce an operating ratio of not greater than 80 each year beginning with
1989. An operating ratio of 802 would ensure sufficient operating revenue to
cover operating costs, debt service, taxes, allocations to the reserve and
staff welfare funds and provide the required internal r0sources for the
investment program, taking into account customer contributions and external
finances. Although the RWW's projected Income Statements (Annex 8.2) show
lower operating ratios in some years, analysis by the appraisal mission
confirmed that at an operating ratio of 80% in each of the years 1989 through
1996, RWW's generation of internal resources would exceed the amounts required
by their financing plan (Table 8.2).
8.09      A detailed schedule of RWW's investment program and financing plan,
for the actual period 1983 to 1987, and for the projected period 1988 to 1996
is set forth in Annex 8.1. A summary of RWW's overall investment program and
financing plan from the start of expenditures for the Bank Project through the
implementation period (1987-1996), in December 1987 Dinars, is provided in
Table 8.2. During the period 1986-1996, annual investment expenditures other
than Bank Project expenditures are projected to average $4.4 million. The
projected external, customer, and internal financing is sufficient to meet
therequirement of the projected investment plan and, consequently, the
financing plan is satisfactory. RWW agreed in negotiations to agree with the
Bank before introducing any increase in a given year's projected expenditures
for investments other than the Bank Project that would cause the annual
average of such expenditures for the period from 1989 through the year in
question to exceed $5.0 million equivalent (15% more than the amount projected
at appraisal).

Istrian Water Works (IWW)
8.10                 Past Performance and Present Position. The actual and projected
Investment Program and Financing Plan, Income Statements, Balance Sheets, and
Statement of Source and Application of Funds from 1983 through 1996, are set
forth in Annexes 8.5, 8.6, 8.7, and 8.8, respectively. Explanations of the
accounts reflected in the financial statements and assumptions for the
projections from 1988 to 1996 are provided in Annex 8.14.
8.11                During the period 1983 to 1987 the operating ratio averaged about
731, ranging between 68% and 79% (Annex 8.6). The self-financing ratio (Annex
8.8) averaged about 55%, varying widely over the 5-year period.  From 731 in
1983, the ratio changed to 262 in 1984, to -31 in 1985, to 111 in 1986, and to
167% in 1987. These wide variances were largely due to large increases in
working capital and debt service (1984-86) relative to the internal funds
generation. In calcu ating the self-financing ratio, increases in



- 46 -
debt-service and working capital are deducted from internal funds generated.
In the case of the year 1985, which reflected a negative self-financing ratio,
debt-service and working capital increases exceeded internal funds
generation. The high self-financing ratio of 167% in 1987 was attributable to
a large increase in customer capital contributions and operating income
resulting from a large increase in tariffs (156%). The rate of return on ,iet
revalued assets amounted to about 7% in 1987, ranging from 5% to 9% over the
1983-87 period. Debt-service coverage ranged between 2.1 and 3.8 for the
period. Overall, the financial performance of IWW was satisfactory for the
1983-87 period.
8.12      The financial position of IWW at the end of 1987 as reflected ir; the
Balance Sheet (Annex 8.7), waE satisfactory. The ratio of debt to debt plus
equity was 11%, while the current ratio was 0.4% due to short-term loans
incurred as bridging finance for the high level of capital expenditure in that
year. The number of months average sales reflected in the 1987 balance of
outstanding accounts receivable amounted to 1.5 months. Owing to the large
tariff increases in the latter peat of 1987, the average month's sales for the
year is considerably lower than the average for November and December of which
the year-end accounts receivable figure is comprised. Consequently, this
indicator is not a valid measure of the conditions of accounts receivable for
this particular year.
8.13      Water Tariffs.  IWW's tariffs in effect at appraisal are shown in
Table 8.3. Customers are billed the basic charge regardless of the amount
consumed; the charged amount loes not cover any partisular quantity of water.
Industrial/commercial customers are granted a discount on the total water
consumed for the calendar year, provided their consumption over ten months of
the year exceeds an average of ,,O0O cu meters per month over the ten-month
period. The discount amounLs to 20% of the Operations charge, and does not
apply to the surcharge for investments. It is rebated at the end of the
calendar year. All five communities served by IWW are charged a uniform
tariff, as shown below. The tariff surcharge for investments is in accordance
with an agreement which terminates December 31, 1991. The operations portion
of the total tariff is frequently increased each year to compensate for the
impact of inflation on operating costs, as well as being seasonally adjusted.
IWW's tariff structure includes a surcharge but lacks a separate charge for
tourism facilities. IWW agreed in negotiations not later than December 31,
1990 to undertake a study of its tariff structure on the basis of terms of
reference satisfactory to the Bank; to consult with the Bank on the findings
and recommendations before March 31, 1991; and promptly thereafter to take
measures to put into effect a revised tariff structure. The study, amongst
other considerations, would address the following:
(i)  separate tariff for establishments providing tourist
accommodation and services; and
(ii)  the discount structure for industrial/commercial customers
and to what degree it oftsets the benefit of the seasonal
surcharge in suppressing demand during the period of annual
peak-demand.
Outline terms of reference for the tariff study are provided in Annex 8.15.

8.14     Future Financial Performance.  During the 1988-96 period covered by
the projections, IWW's operating ratio (Annex 8.6) is projected to decrease
from about 712 in 1987 to 382 in 1988, and to increase to 74% in 1996. This
indicator reaches a peak of 77% in 1994 owing to an increase of about 1581 in
depreciation attributable to the transfer of the proposed Bank Project assets
from Butoniga, the project implementing organization, to IWW's assets in
operation during that year. Due to start-up of the new production facilities,
and the gradual increase in customer demand, the full sales income potential
of the added production capacity will not be realized during the projected
period. The operating ratio, however, is projected to fall to about 741 by
1996. The rate of return on net revalued fixed assets (Annex 8.6) is
projected to increase from about 7% in 1987 to 29% in 1988 and thereafter to
decrease to about 4% by 1996. The self-financing ratio (Annex 8.8), measuring
the percentage of capital expenditure covered by internally generated funds
and customers contributions, is projected to be about 621 in 1988, decreasing
to 27% in 1994, and increasing to 86% in 1996, owing to the signiticant
decrease in the investment program.  Debt service coverage, by internally
generated resources, is projected at about 13 (times debt service) in 1988,
decreasing to 1.4 by 1996.  Although the rate of return on net revalued assets
is somewhat low for the latter three years of the nine year projected period,
the self-financing ratio indicates a significantly high level of internal cash
generation to ensure that the internal funding of the investment program is
adequately covered, to ensure that the Reserve Fund and Welfare Fund
allocation requirements are met, and to permit an expansion of the investment
program in the future, if needed. The projected financial performance of IWW
is satisfactory.
8.15     The projected financial position of IWW, as reflected in the balance
sheet (Annex 8.7), indicates a satisfactory condition. The ratio of debt to
equity plus debt reaches the high point of 251 in 1992, attributable to the
drawdown of the proposed Bank loan, but decreases eacii year thereafter
reaching the level of 171 in 1996. The current ratio ranges between 1.8 to
8.4 over the 1988-96 period. Accounts receivable in terms of number of
months' average sales range from a high of 1.5 months in 1987 to 1 month in
1996.



- 48 -
8.16     IWW agreed in negotiations to maintain tariffs at a sufficient level
to ensure an operating ratio of not greater than 80% each year beginning with
1989. An operating ratio of 80% would ensure sufficient operating revenue to
cover operating costs, debt service, taxes, allocations to the Reserve and
Staff Welfare Funds and provide the required internal resources for the
investment program, taking into account customer capital contributions and
external financing. Although the IWW's projected Income Statements (Annex
8.6) show lower operating ratios in some years, analysis by the appraisal
mission confirmed that at an operating ratio of 80% in each of the years 1989
to 1996, IWW's generation of internal resources would exceed the amounts
required by their financing plan (Table 8.4).
8.17     A schedule of IWW's investment program and financing plan, for the
actual period 1983 to 1987 and for the projected period 1988 to 1996, is
provided in Annex 8.5. A summary of IWW's overall investment program and
financing plan, from the start of capital expenditures for the Bank Project in
1987 until 1996, priced in December 1987 Dinars, is provided in Table 8.4.
During the period 1989-1996, annual investment expenditures other than Bank
Project expenditures are projected to average $2.2 million. The projected
external, customer, and internal financing is sufficient to meet the
requirement of the projected investment plan and, consequently, the financing
plan is satisfactory.  IWW agreed in negotiations to agree with the Bank
before iatroducing any increase in a given year's projected expenditures for
investments other than the Bank Project that would cause the annual average of
such expenditures for the period from 1989 tlrough the year in question to
exceed $2.5 million equivalent (15% more than the amount projected at
appraisal).
Pula Water Works (PWW)
8.18     Past Performance and Present Position. The actual and projected
Investment and Financing Plan, Income Statements, Balance Sheets, and
Statements of Source and Application of Funds from 1983 to 1996 are provided
ir. Annexes 8.9, 8.10, 8.11 and 8.12, respectively. Explanations of the
accounts reflected in the financial statements and assumptions for the
projections from 1988 to 1996 are provided in Annex 8.14.
8.19     During the period 1983 to 1987, PWW's after tax income was low.  This
was reflected in their operating ratio (Annex 8.10) which was 92%, 891, 87%,
73X and 801 for each year, respectively. This was due to an insufficient
level of tariffs. With a substantial increase in tariffs in 1987, PWW
generated a net after tax income of Dinar 510 million and achieved an
operating ratio of 80X. The rate of return on net revalued assets ranged from
1.42 to 3.1% from 1983 to 1985 but increased substantially to 8.1% and 6.6% in
1986 and 1987 respectively owing to a large increase in tariffs in both of
these years. PWW's self-financing ratio was high from 1983 to 1986, averaging
611, attributed to a very low level of capital expenditures. This indicator
was 240% for 1987, due to the increase in tariffs and an infusion of
contributed capital of Dinar 12 billion from customers. Debt service was
satisfactorily covered during the entire period 1983-87 due to a low level of
borrowed capital.  The financial performance was not satisfactory in the years
1983, 1984 and 1985, however, with the increase in tariffs and additional
equity capital the financial performance in 1986 and 1987 is considered
satisfactory.

8.20             The financial position of PWW, as reflected in the balance sheet
(Annex 8.11), was satisfactory from 1983 to 1987. The ratio of debt to debt

8.21            Water Tariffs.  PWW's tariffs in effect at appraisal are shown in
Table 8.5. There are no basic or minimum charges, nor discounts for large
volume customers. The tariffs below are applied to all customers in the Pula
Conmmunity which PWW serves. Tariffs are increased frequently to compensate



- 50 -
for the impact of inflation on PWW's operating costs, and are seasonally
adjusted to discourage excessive consumption during the period of
peak-demand. The seasonal increase, however, applies to the investment
surcharge and not to the operations part of the tariffs. As with RWW and IWW,
PWW's tariff structure could be improved also. PWW agreed in negotiations,
not later than December 31, 1990, to undertake a study of its tariff structure
on the basis of terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank; to consult with
the Bank on the findings and recommendations before March 31, 1991; and to
promptly thereafter take measures to put into effect a revised tariff
structure. The study, amongst other considerations, would address the
following:
(i) seasonal surcharge on the operations part of the
industrial/commercial, and residential tariffs;
{ii) Separate tariff for establishments providing tourist
accommodations and services; and,
(iii) a minimum monthly charge to all classes oi customers.
outline terms of reference for the tariff study are provided in Annex 8.15

8.22     Future Financid Performance.  During the 1988 to 1996 period, PWW's
operating ratio in 1988 through 1990 is projected to remain constant at about
47%. It is projected to increase to 61% in 1991, largely due to dropping the
surcharge for investments from the tariff in that year, while in 1992 and 1993
it decreases to 52%. In 1994 this ratio is projected to increase to 791 due
to the considerably higher depreciation resulting from the assets of the
proposed Project being taken over from Butoniga and transferred to assets in
operation. For 1995 and 1996, the operating ratio remains at about 79%. The
rate of return on net revalued fixed assets is projected at 271 to 28% from
1988 to 1990, 18-241 from 1991-93, and 41 to 31 from 1994 to 1996, reflecting
the impact of depreciation on the assets of the proposed Project. The debt
service coverage ratio is projected at 489 in 1988, decreasing to a level of
about 2 from 1994 to 1996 due to the commencement of debt service payments for
the proposed Bank loan. The high debt service ratio from 1988-93 (489-570) is
due to the very low projected level of loan financing during this period. The
projections reflect a self-financing ratio ranging from a low of 431 in 1988



- 51 -
to a high of 1022 in 1996. With the exception of 1988 and 1991, in all nine
years of the period of projections the self-financing ratio is 622 or higher.
The financial performance as projected for the period 1988 to 1996 is
satisfactory.
8.23     PWW's projected financial position from 1988 to 1996, as reflected in
the balance sheet (Annex 8.11) is satisfactory. The ratio of debt to debt
plus equity reaches its high point at about 201 in 1992, and thereafter
decreases consistently each year to 1996 at which time it is projected to be
13%. The current ratio ranges between 3 and 11 over the nine year period of
projections. Outstanding accounts receivable measured in terms of avurage
months' sales is projected to be under one month average sales for each of the
years 1988 to 1996. The projected financial position of PWW is satisfactory.
8.24     PWW agreed in negotiations to maintain tariffs at a sufficient level
to ensure an operating ratio of not greater than 801 each year beginuing with
1989. An operating ratio of 80% would ensure sufficient operating revenue to
cover operating costs, debt service, taxes on income, allocations to the
Reserve and Staff Welfare funds and provide the required internal resources
for the investment program, taking into account customer capital contributions
and external financing. Although the PWW's projected Income Statements (Annex
8.10) show lower operating ratios in some years, analysis by the appraisal
mission confirmed that at an operating ratio of 801 in each of the years 1989
to 1996, PWW's generation of internal resources would exceed the amounts
required by their financing plan (Table 8.6).

annual average of such expenditures for the period from 1989 through the year
in question to exceed $2.0 million equivalent (151 more than the amount

Audit
8.26     The annual financial statements of RWW, IWW, and PWW are subject to
audit by the Federal Social Accounting Services (SDK). A special accounting
group with SDK is able to translate and reclassify the financial statements of
the three Water Works in accordance with the International Accounting
Committee Standards. The three Water Works agreed in negotiations that,
beginning with the 1989 fiscal year, they would have SDK's special accounting
group translate and reclassify their financial statements. Furthermore the
three Water Works agreed that all audited statements and the related audit
reports would be submitted to the Bank within eight months of the end of each
fiscal year.

Monitoring and Evaluation
8.27              The three Water Works agreed in negotiations to submit to the Bank
semiannual reports (due March 15 and September 15) on their financial
performance during the preceding semesters ending December 31 and June 30.
The reports would be in the formats of the financial statements included in
this report (Annexes 8.1 to 8.12) and would compare actual performancewith
projections. Explanations of variances of the actual figures from the
projections would be provided, together with analysis of the causes of the
differences from projections of the following monitoring indicators:



- 53 -
(i) Operating ratio
(ii) Rate of return on net revalued fixed assets
(iii) Debt service coverage
(iv) Self-financing ratio
(v) Ratio of long-term debt to debt plus equity
(vi) Accounts receivable in number of months average sales
These reports would be in addition to the audit reports prepared by SDK
(para. 8.26), and would be submitted together with the report on project
implementation (para. 7.43).



- 54 -
DC. ECONOMIC EVALUATION AND RESKS
Benefits
9.01     The proposed water supply investments to be made under the Project
are necessary to provide an adequate supply of potable water to the
households, productive enterprises, and social facilities, including
hospitals, in the project area; all of which have experienced insufficiencies
since 1983. Because of the importance of tourism in the area to both the
local and national economies, however, the economic justification for the
Project is based primarily on the foreign exchange earnings of tourism. The
proposed investments in water supply are necessary to avoid decreases in
occupancy in existing hotels, and to allow further development of hotels and
other tourist facilities according to the development plans for the area.
9.02     Since the project is only part of a larger program of investments
required to expand tourism capacity, the economic evaluation has been made in
terms of this larger program. The gross benefits of the investments consist
mainly of the additional revenues earned or "saved" 1' by hotel and other
facilities used by tourists. Investment costs include not only the capital
required to construct the proposed water investments, but also the costs of
other necessary infrastructure (primarily sewage disposal facilities), and the
construction costs of the additional tourism facilities. The net benefits of
the program of investments would be the additional revenues generated or
"saved" as a result of increased tourism in the project area, less the costs
of operating the facilities and services to be provided (Annex 9.1 gives
details on the assumptions underlying the economic rate of return
calculations.)
Rates of Return
9.03     The estimated economic rate of return on the tourism-related
investment program, including the proposed Project, is 15X. The results of
sensitivity tests, carried out for the effects of changes in occupancy rate,
exchange rates, and delays in hotel construction, indicate that the returns
are not expected to drop below 10% under a combination of adverse conditions.
Log) Run Average Incremental Cost of Water
9.04     The long run average incremental cost (AIC) of water for each of the
Water Works was calculated for comparison with their tourism tariff at the end
of the Project (see Table 9.1 and Annex 9.1). The results show that the AIC
of water exceeds the tariff projected for IWW and PWW but is less than the
tariff for RWW. The reason for the RWW tariffs and AIC being significantly
higher than the IWW and PlWW tariffs and AIC is the two phase transfer of the
RWW's Gradole system capacity from RWW to IWW and PWW in 1995 and 2005. The
current tariff structures for IWW and PWW need to be adjusted so that the
1/ "Saved" means revenues of existing hotels which in the absence of the
Project might be lost through lower occupanciec.



- 55 -
tourism industry pays the AIC of water. Tariff studies to be undertaken by
the three Water Works would consider the AIC as one factor in establishing a
new tariff structure (paras. 8.05, 8.13 and 8.21).
Table 9.1: Comparison of Projected Water Tariffs
with Average Incremental Costs a/

a/ Tariffs are for 1994, the year the Project is completed.
b/ Assumes current tariff structure which sets the tourism tariff at
two times the domestic tariff.
Risks
9.05      There is a high degree of confidence the Project will provide the
desired benefits and will be implemented on schedule. There is little
physical risk involved with the implementation of the program. Most of the
project works are of conventional design and well within the capability of
local contractors who are most likely to win the civil works contracts. The
project also has some financial risks, principally possibilities of failure of
the Borrowers to adjust tariffs unward with inflation, and of cost overruns in
program implementation. These risks are minimal because of the substantial
effort that has gone into the technical preparation and the financial planning
of the Project and the importance attached to it by the beneficiaries and the
governments of the two republics.
9.06      There are only two risks of real concern.  One is the risk of
possible damage to the area's tourism development if the water shortages are
not managed properly until the Project is completed. At the present time, the
available water is being distributed equitably and efficiently. The Bank
would try to minimize this risk by monitoring operational procedures of the
three Water Works as part of its supervision. The other is a risk that the
overall investment climate in Yugoslavia could deteriorate further and become
a constraint to tourism development in the project area. However, the demand
for tourism services is likely to remain strong even in the presence of
negative macro-economic trends.



- 56 -
X. AGREEMENIS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
10.01    RWW, IWW and PWW agreed in negotiations that they would:
(i)    enter into agreements with the entities implementing the
sewerage works included in the Project (para. 7.21);
(ii)    carry out tariff studies included in the Project on the basis of
teams of reference satisfactory to the Bank not later than
December 31., 1990, consult the Bank on the recommendations and
findings before March 31, 1991 and take measures for their
implementation promptly thereafter (paras. 8.05, 8.13 and 8.21);
(iii)    maintain annual operating ratios of not greater than 80%
beginning with 1989 (paras. 8.08, 8.16, and 8.24);
(iv)    agree with the Bank before including in a given years investment
program for investments other than the Bank Project expenditures
that would cause average annual expenditures in the 1989-1996
period to exceed $5.0 million equivalent for RWW, $2.5 million
equivalent for IWW and $2.0 million equivalent for PWW (other
investment expenditures for each Water Works projected at
appraisal plus 15X) (paras. 8.09, 8.17 and 8.25); and
(v)    submit to the Bank reports on their financial performances
(para. 8.27), project implementation (para. 7.43),
administration of Special Accounts (paras. 7.39 and 7.40), use
of Statements of Expenditures (paras. 7.38 and 7.40), project
completion (para. 7.43) and their annual audit (para. 8.26).
10.02    Furthermore, IWW and PWW agreed that they would:
(i)    through the Committee for Water Management of the Republic of
Croatia complete a Sewerage Master Plan Study for Central Istria
on the basis of terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank not
later than December 31, 1990 (para. 7.24); and
(ii)    except RWW, carry out a management study of water supplies in
Istria component project area (including the Project water
works) on the basis of terms of reference satisfactory to the
Bank not later than December 31, 1992, consult the Bank on the
recommendations before March 31, 1993 and take measures to
obtain the approval of the agreed recommendations by the
appropriate authorities promptly thereafter (para. 7.31).
10.03    And RWW and PWW agreed that they would carry out action plans for the
reduction of unaccounted-for water by December 31, 1994 (para. 5.17).
10.04    As a condition of effectiveness, RWW, IWW and PWW agreed to provide
the signed agreements referred to in 10.01(i).



- 57 -
10.05    With the above agreements and conditions, the proposed ProJect would
be suitable for Bank loans totalling $60 million equivalent ($32 million to
RWW and $28 million to IWW and PWW). Each loan would be for a period of 15
years, including five years of grace, at the standard variable interest rate.

SOCIALIST FEDERAL REPUBIIC OF YUGOSLAVIA
ISTRIA AND SLOVENE COAST WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Water Demand and Production Requirements
1.       Detailed historical and projected water production for the RWW, IWW
and PWW in table and graphic format are presented in this Annex. The tables
include all the indicators used in projecting water consumption and production
requirements and making comparisons with other water companies. Superimposed
on the graphs are lines showing historical production capacity since 1980 and
the capacity available after the project is complete.
2.       The graphs demonstrate the adequacy of the production by comparing it
with maximum day requirements. Future increases in production capacity even
though planned are not shown in order to emphasize when additional capacity
would be needed with the Project. In all cases the works under the Project
are expandable to meet future needs until 2015. According to the graphs,
capacity provided under the Project would meet projected production
requirements for the RWW until 2005, the IWW until 1998 and the PWW until
1997. The small changes in production capacity occurring in 1995 and 2005 in
all three graphs are the result of reallocation of the Gradole water source
capacity which takes place in these years.

LSTRIA AND SLOVENE COAST WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
Action Plan for Rizana Water Works to Reduce Unaccounted for Water
Completion Date
1.   Establish a daily and weekly control                 December 1989
program to monitor changes in the
system that could indicate water loss.
2.   Establish a program for the weekly                   December 1989
inspection of large meters and the
repair of meters that are found to
be malfunctioning.
3.   Evaluate existing large meters for                   June 1990
accuracy and adapt new standards for
procurement if justified.
4.   Carry out a water balance of the                     December 1990
distribution system to identify
the major source of wastage and
set priorities for its reduction.
5.   Design a leak detection program and                  December 1991
organize and equip a leak detection
team.
6.   Establish 30 metered waste control                   December 1992
districts for the monitoring of distri-
bution losses and design and implement
a program for monitoring water sales
by district.
7.   Install new control systems at                      December 1992
pumping stations to control pump
operation by tank water levels.
8.   Inspect all customer connections for                 December 1993
leakage and replace fittings that have
performed poorly in the past.
9.   Install a control center to monitor                  December 1994
and operate the distribution network.

10.  Replace 40 km of old and leaky pipes.              December 1994

ANNEX 7.1
Page 1
SOCIALIST FEDERAL REPUBLIC OF YUGOSLAVIA
ISTRIA AND SLOVENE COAST WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
Outline Terms of Reference for a Central Istria Master Plan Sewerage Study
Backgrund
1.       Central Istria has lagged behind the rest of Istria in adequate
provisions for the disposal of sewage. The coastal communities all have
adequate master plans and most of the works design to protect the tourism
areas from marine pollution have already been implemented. Pollution from
combined sewer overflows and sanitary sewers not connected to interceptors
still exists in the waters near the old city centers of Novigrad, Rovinj and
Pula. Improvements to correct the problems in these places have been
identified and included in the 1986-1990 plan. Whereas the city center of the
inland community of Buzet has a secondary treatment plant with 80% of the
service area population connected and the Buje city center has an adequate
system planned, the treatment and disposal of sewage from Pazin city center
remains an unsolved problem and the smaller town centers either rely on septic
tanks or direct discharge to the nearest water course after only preliminary
treatment. The Pazia problem is the most serious since some of the local
industries do not pretreat their sewage. Traces of their sewage has been
detected in the potable water source dowrstream of Pazin Center which serves a
total population of 60,000 in Labin and Pula. A study carried out in 1986
recommended a solution which was not acceptable to the neighboring communities.
Objectives
2.       The objectives of the study are:  (a) determine the adequacy of
existing treatment and disposal methods in Central Istria; (b) recommend a
solution for the Pazin Center sewage disposal problem that is both acceptable
and affordable; and (c) estimate the sewage disposal needs for the rest of
Central Istria.
Study Area
3.       The study area is Central Istria which is defined as the area
comprising the communities of Buzet and Pazin and the non-coastal areas of the
communities of Buje, Porec, Rovinj and Pula.
Scope of Work
4.       The study has the following scope of work:
(a) Based on existing development plans and past trends, project the
sewage treatment End disposal requirement of the study area to
the year 2015.
(b) rake a reconnaissance of sewage disposal practices and facilities
for urban centers with 500 population or more in the study area
and determine where improvements are required to meet current and



- 77 -
ANNEX 7.1
Page 2
(c) Make an industrial waste survey of the study area to determine
the extent of industrial pollution.
(e) Recommend solutions for areas where there is a need for new or
expanded facilities to meet (i) current needs, and (ii) future
needs.
(f) Determine the extent to which the sewage from Pazin is polluting
the water supply sources for Pula and Labin.
(g) Review past recommendations for the treatment and disposal of
sewage from the Pazin city center and the nearby industries and
propose alternatives, including the optior of primary treatment
and marine disposal by sea outfall.
(h) Make an assessment of non-point source pollution in the study
area and recommend control measures.
(i) Recommend and plan for meeting the waste disposal needs of the
study area. Include costs and proposals for financing and
implementation.
(j) Propose a program for the monitoring and control of waste
discharges in the study area including both domestic and
industrial wastes.
(k) Study institutional arrangements for the treatment and disposal
of sewage in the study area, including the coastal communes.
Include as one alternative a regional organization.
(1) Review existing waste water discharge regulations in the study
area and propose new regulations if the existing ones are not
censidered adequate.
(m) Review existing procedures for the monitoring and enforcement of
existing waste water discharge regulations in the study area and
recommend changes that may be required to enforce any new
regulations recommended in item (1) or ensure compliance with
existing regulations if the review of the existing regulations
finds them to be sufficient.

SOCIALIST FEDERAL REPUBLIC OF YUGOSLAVIA
ISTRIA AND SLOVENE COAST WATER SITPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
Project Cost Estimates
1.       Summary cost estimates by expenditure category, year and financing
source are included in Tables A7.1 through A7.8 of this Annex. All costs are
presented in US dollars. Projections of current costs in Dinars is difficult
because of the high inflation rates being experienced in Yugoslavia. Detailed
estimates are included in the Project File. All cost estimates were prepared
using December 31, 1987 prices and an exchange rate of Din 1,344 to 1. The
base costs in the tables have been updated to December 31, 1988 prices.
2.       Bid prices have been used where available.  The civil works cost
estimates are based on preliminary quantity estimates and unit prices of
similar work in Yugoslavia and other countries in the Region. Pipe,
equipment, and material costs are based on supplier quotations. Land costs
are based on estimates furnished by Water Works' estimators.
3.       The cost of consultants for design, construction supervision, and
technical assistance are based on man-month estimates and rates chargea by
foreign and local consultants in Yugoslavia. Local staff eosts have not been
included.
4.       Taxes consist of import duties on equipment expected to be imported.
An average rate of 21X h6'. been used. Rates vary in practice, but past
experience has shown that 21% is the normally expected average for water works
projects. No other taxes would be charged to the Project.
5.       Physical contingencies are 0 percent for consultants, staff salaries
and land and 10 percent for equipment, materials and civil works. Price
contingency factors and factors for updating base costs estimates assumed the
following inflation rates:
Year                Inflation (%)
1988-89                 3.0
1990                    3.5
1991-95                 4.0

Page 1
SOCIALIST FEDERAL REPUBLIC OF YUGOSLAVIA
ISTRIA AND SLOVENTE COAST WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
Outline Terms of Reference for a Management Study of Water Supplies in Istria
Backgound
1.       Three water works provide water supply to seven communities in the
Istria peninsula, the Istrian Water Works, the Pula Water Works and the Labin
Water Works (LWW). The Istrian Water Works (IWW) and the Pula Water Works
(PWW) already share a c^mmon supply and transmission system, the Gradole
system; and after completion of the Project, will share the Butoniga system.
The Gradole system is operated and maintained by the Istrian Water Works. The
situation with the new system is not clear. It is being constructed by the
Butoniga Water Works, a special project organization with legal responsibility
only to construct the system, not operate it. When it is complete, it would
be turned over to the owners, the IWW and the PWW. The LWW has no shared
facilities with the IWW and PWW and is expected to remain independent of them
in the foreseeable future.
2.       Several alternatives exist for the operation of the Butoniga System.
These are: (a) have one of the two water works operate the system as with the
Gradole system; (b) let the Butoniga Water Works remain in existence and
operate the system as a separate organization, selling water to the two water
works and possibly taking over the Gradole system also; (c) privatize the
system; and (d) combining the Istrian and Pula Water Works into a single
organization that would own and operate all water facilities in the two
service areas under a single authority. Alternative (d), the combining of the
two water works has been considered in the past and is still under discussion.
Objectives
3.       The objectives of the study are:  (a) to determine the costs and
benefits of combining the IWW and PWW to decide if such an objective is worthy
of further consideration; and (b) to determine the best method of operating
and maintaining the jointly owned water supply systems in Istria in the long
term and the Butoniga system in the short term.
Scope of Work
3.       The study would have the following scope of work:
(a) Design alternatives for the future organization and management
of water supplies in the service areas of the IWW and PWM' For
each alternative: (i) determine personnel required; (ii) new
facilities; and cost of operation over a 10 year period
beginning in 1990. The alternatives should include, but not be
limited to the following the alternatives listed in paragraph
2. Each alternative should consider the immediate operational
requirements of the Butoniga system.



- 93 -
ANNEX 7.8
Page 2
(b) Compare the discounted costs and benefits of the various
alternatives.
(c) Analyze the legal aspects of the alternatives to determine if
the proposals are in conflict with any existing laws or
regulations and if so, could they be changed.
(d) Identify and analyze the political constraints to each
alternative.
(e) Recommend an the best alternative and prepare an implementation
plan and schedule.

ANNEX 8.13
SOCIALIST FEDERAL REPUBLIC OF YUGOSLAVIA
ISTRIA AND SLOVENE COAST WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Accounting Practices in Yugoslavia
1.       Operating Expenses represent all cob-s and expenses incurred in the
production process and for services rendered during each applicable fiscal
year.
2.       Operating Revenue.  In accordance with Yogoslav accounting
regulations, sales and related credits are only recorded as revenues when
payment, or a guarantee of payment, is received by December 31 of the current
year or, in respect of goods dispatched and services rendered within the last
15 days of the financial year, when payment or a bill of exchange is received
within the first 15 days of the following year. In all other instances the
amounts billed but not received are carried forward and treated as revenue in
the period in which they are collected.
3.       Interest Income and Investment Income are not accrued but are
credited to income when received.
4.       Interest Payable is accrued, to the balance sheet date, and charged
against revenue.
5.       Net Fixed Assets and Construction in Progress are revalued annually
to approximate replacement cost. Revaluation coefficients are applied to both
gross fixed assets and to accumulated depreciation. Revaluation coefficients
are annually established by Government for a wide range of asset
classifications, based upon current market prices, and are appropriately
applied and are satisfactory. The net surplus resulting from revaluation is
credited direct to the Business Fund component of equity.
6.       Accounts Receivable include all amounts owing for sales and services
rendered, including those which cannot be recognized as sales under Yugoslav
law (para. 2).
7.       Losses Arising from Revaluation of Foreign Currency Loans, and
repayment of principal of such loans, are included as a part of the
revaluation of the fixed assets for which they were incurred.

Explanation of the Accounts Reflected in the Financial
Statements and Assumptions for the Financial Projections
RIZANA WATERWORKS
Income Statement
1.       Water Production - after project demand forecasts (Annex 5.1).
2.       Water Distribution Losses - after project demand forecasts
(Annex 5.1).
3.       Tariffs - increased each year 1988-91, and held relatively constant
from 1992-96.
4.       Service Income - represents water connections and other works
chargeable to customers, and is projected to remain constant from 1988 to 1996
at the 1988 figure of Dinar 1,751 million.
5.       Salaries, Wages and Staff Benefits - includes RWW salaries and wage
related costs, and is projected to increase to in the ratio of increase in the
number of employees but at the same cost per employee that prevailed in
December 1987.
6.       Electricity - projected to increase at about 2% per year, at December
1987 prices, from 1988-96.
7.       Materials, Chemicals and Spare Parts - projected to increase by about
1% per year at December 1987 prices.
8.       Water Purchases from Istria - represents water purchases from Istria
(Gradole system), and is projected at quantity required to meet customer
demand up to the allocated limit established by the Gradole Agreement with
Istria Water Works. RWW's costs reflect the ratio of operating cost sharing
established by the above agreement, which are priced in the projections at
December 1987 costs.
9.       Maintenance Contracted - represents maintenance work performed by
outside contractors, and is projected to meet RWW's maintenance program.
Projections reflect a substantial increase from 1994 to 1996 which is due to
the planned renewal of segments of the distribution system (para 2).



- 112 -
Annex 8.14
Page 2
10.      Provision for Depreciation - projected at the minimum rates
prescribed by Government plus supplementary arbitrary amoun.s determined by
the investment program needs for internal financing. The purpose for this
practice is that capital expenditure from internal resources is restricted to
the annual provision for depreciation, plus the annual allocatioa of net
income after taxes to the Business Fund.
11.      Obligatory Contribution to Communities - includes premiums for RWW's
insurance coverage, contribution and levies for civil defense, social welfare,
education, health services, science, culture, social security, unemployment
insurance, environmental agencies, communal activities, and Self-Management
Communities of Interest (SIS). Projections are proportionate to the increase
in the "Income Base." The "Income Base" is defined in Yugoslavia as operating
income plus salaries and wages, and obligatory contribution to communities.
12.      Other Operating Expenses - represents travel expenses, inventory
shortages, bank service charges, computer software and supplies, contractor's
charges related to Service Income (para. 4), purchases of assets of small
value, laboratory analysis costs, inventory revolution, revaluation losses,
interest on short-term loans and credits,and other miscellaneous expenses.
Projections reflect nominal increases of about 1.92 per year, December 1987
prices.
13.      Interest on Loans and Interest During Construction - self-explanatory
and projected according to the terms of the proposed Bank loan and domestic
currency long-term loans, as reflected in loan schedules.
14.      Taxes on Income - represents income taxes levied by the Republic of
Slovenia, and is projected at approximately 2.7% of the "Income Base"
explained in para. 11 above.
Balance Sheet
15.      Fixed Assets and Provision for Depreciation - are revalued annually
to approximate replacement costs (Annex 8.13, para 5).
16.      Long-term Deposits/Investments - represents a large number of loans
and deposits ad-;anced to various institutions involved in such areas as power
generation and development, telephone services, underdeveloped areas, civil
defense, culture, etc. Projections are based on arbitrary judgment.
17.      Cash & Bank, and short-term deposits/loans - projected to meet
liquidity needs and to absorb liquid surplus generated by projected operations.
18.      Accounts Receivable - represents outstanding invoices for water
sales and services rendered. Due to RWW's stringent and effective collection
practices accounts receivable are projected to remain at the 1987 level, in
absolute terms, throughout the 1988-96 projection period.



- 113 -
Annex 8.14
Page 3
19.      Inventories - Projected to continue at approximately the 1987 level
from 1988 to 1996.
20.      Other Current Assets   represents housing and other loans to staff.
Projections based on past experience and arbitrary judgment of availability of
resources and staff future needs.
21.      Reserve Fund - represents a reserve of earned "after tax" income to
protect the Equity of RWW in the event of operating deficits in future years
and to absorb losses on the disposal of assets. Net income after taxes is
allocated to the Reserve Fund at the rate of 4% of the "Income Base" (para.
11) up to the level where Reserve Fund balance equals the average "income
base" for the previous three years.
22.      Staff Welfare Fund - represents an annual allocation of net after tax
income and establishes the level of funding available to pay staff welfare
benefits, such as staff seniority allowances, holiday allowances, retirement
allowances, staff vacation houses and caravans, and electric, water, etc.
services related to vacation accommodation, regular (non-vacation) staff
housing, and community housing (non-staff). The calculat'ons of the
allocation to this fund are many and complex. Projections for 1988-96 have
been calculated applying the approximate formulae.
23.      Capital Contributions: Communities - represents contributions from
the Slovenia Association of Water Communities, and customer contributions
collected and forwarded through the communities operating on a lower-than
republic level. The original source of such non-republic contributions is
from tourist taxes, industrial and commercial water consumers, and the
Community of Interest for Water of Koper, whose contributions are derived from
industrial and commercial enterprises.
24.      Solidarity Fund - represents annual allocations of net after tax
income segregated to provide funding for community and republic emergencies
and natural and not-so-natural disasters. The projections are based on
complex esoteric formulae.
25.      Business Fund - represents the remaining net income after taxes,
after allocations to the Reserve, Staff Welfare and Solidarity Funds, losses
resulting from the revaluation of assets and accumulative depreciation, and
direct contributions from water customers. Capital expenditure financed from
RWW's internal resources is restricted by law to the net after tax income
allocated to this fund, plus the annual provision for depreciation charged to
revenue.
26.      Accounts Payable - is self-explanatory and has been projected in
proportion to change in inventories and the increase in materials, chemicals,
supplies and spare parts consumed in operations.



- 114 -
Annex 8.14
Page 4
27.      Short-term Loans & Credits - represents short-term borrowing for
working capital needs. Projections have been made on the assumption that this
liability will remain at the 1987 level for the 1988-96 period projected.
28.      Other Current Liabilities - represents accrued payroll and other
accrued expenses, and many small loans to water customers, community
associations, etc. Projections are based on arbitrary judgment for this item.
29.      Added at Year-end Revaluation - This item, under both long-term
liability and current liabilities represents the increase in both categories
of liabilities resulting from the planned revaluation of loans payable,
expected to be mandatory by law in Yugoslavia before the end of 1988. The
balance sheet reflects no figures for this item as the revaluation requirement
did not exist for the actual period 1983 to 1987, and for the 1988 to 1996
projected period it does not apply as projections based on December 1987
prices, consequently, excluding inflation from these figures. This item has,
nevertheless, been included in the balance sheet so that the format will
provide for revaluation which will be included in the actual operating results
which should be submitted by RWW to the Bank for the years 1988-96.

ANNEX 8.15

SOCIALIST FEDERAL REPUBLIC OF YUGOSLAVIA
1STRIA AND SLOVENE COAST WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE PROJECT
Outline Terms of Ref -,ene for a Tariff Study
Background
1.       The          Water Works is undertaking a program of expansion of its
water supply capacity inorder to meet the continuing growth in water demand
for its service area. E.cisting capacity is sufficient for average
requirements but is insufficient for the peak summertime use which results
mostly from the influx of tourists and vacationers. Economic analysis has
demonstrated that the income from tourism justifies meeting the demand rather
than discouraging its growth so as not to have to invest in the additional
capacity. Having made this decision, tariffs should be set so that there is
equity in the distribution of the cost of service between the different
categories of consumers in terms of capital.
Objctive
2.       The objective of the study is to develop a tariff structure that
would take into consideration but not be limited to the following:
(a) The long run average incremental cost of water.
(b) Affordability. The domestic water and sewerage charge should be
less than 5S of the minimum household income for the resident
population.
(c) Equity in the distribution of cost of service between the
various categories of userr in the economy (industry, commercial
and tourism).
(d) Financial needs of the water works to cover the cost of
providing the service, full debt service payment, legal and
contractual taxes and contributions, appropriate increases to
working capital, and capital requirements for minor expansion of
facilities not financed by developers, the eventual consumers,
or by other means.
(e) Water conservation. Because of limited sources of supply and
the high level of unaccounted-for water (RWW and PWW only),
consideration should be given to systems of charges that would
both discourage excessive usage and encourage user system
maintenance.
(f) Seasonality of demand. Systems of charges should encourage a
more even use of water and discourage use during the peak summer
months.



- 119 -
ANNEX 8.15
Page 2
(g) Other potential forms of revenue generation that may reduce the
revenue requirement from tariffs.
Scope of Work
3.       The study would have the following scope of work:
(a) Calculate the average incremental cost of water based on Butoniga
and future sources of supply.
(b) Calculate the unit cost of capacity for Butoniga and future
source development.
(c) Allocate capital and operating cost per cubic meter sold
according the time of year.
(d) Meters are read monthly. Determine the cost of meter reading and
the benefits of more frequent reading during the peak summer
months (Theoretically, peaks recorded for summer time users
should be higher if meters are read at shorter intervals of time).
(e) Design a computer model for testing the yield of alternative
tariff structures based on historical consumption patterns.
(f) Propose other methods of revenue generation such as connection
fees and taxes from frontage assessment to recover benefits from
increased land values.
(g) Design and test alternative tariff structures taking into account
the results of (f) and recommend one that best meets the
objectives of the study.
(h) Estimate the cost of implementation and develop an implementation
plan and schedule for the recommended tariff structure.



- 120 -
ANNEX 9.1
